Fish ! Fisb.! Fish !
PAWING to perfect arrangements in the FISH and GAME
DEPARTMENT, the Company is now supplying FISH
to an enormous section of the inhabitants of the city, and
residents in every country town of N.S.W.— as a matter
of fact, to many towns-outside, notably Brisbane.
A telegraph order from any country town despatched
before 1 p.m. (provided the Telegraph Department do
their part) is certain of execution by mail train same day,
and as Pish is carried at half parcels rates- equal to about
id. per lb. for the most distant towns—the luxury of a
fish diet is within the reach of all, the F.F. & I. Co.’s
prices (free on rail) being extremely moderate. We pre-
pay trainage, thereby effecting a saving of 50 percent.
The Company stock all kinds of New Zealand and N.S.
Wales fish, also some American kinds—notably Salmon—
and arrangements have been concluded with the Orient
Line to fit a steamer. The first consignment of English
fish and game leaves London sth May, 1893.
Price-lists obtainable at Company’s Depot, 135 King-st,
or from the Manager, 25 to 33 Harbour-street.
Note.— We shall be pleased for country visitors to in-
spect our refrigerating establishment at Harbour-street.
The Company deliver daily to all parts of the city and
suburbs—Milk, Ice, Cream, Butter, Poultry, Fish, Game,
Pastry, &c.
Hy. Fatesou, Manager.

THE Belle Sagamodu.
IIIAD always detested Richardson,
although wc were ostensibly friends,
but my dislike for him culminate !
when he cut me oat ovith the Avealthy
M s. Macphcrsoli. Then I felt that
the world AA-as far too small for both
of us. All my carefully-devised
schemes of vengeance, hoAveA’er, re*
colled upon myself. Richardson laid
the luck of the devil.
We AVere both engaged in tho South Sea trade,
and R'chardson, alas! avos also engaged to the
AvidoAV. This avos to be our final A'oyage together,
fo- Richardson was about to “ settle down. ’
Of course, on our last trip avo must needs come
to utter grief : one morning avg fiund ou selves a
couple of wretched trade’s on Sagarnodu Island,
and, worse still, in the hands of the Batta-battas,
the most ferocious cannibals south of the equator.
But it so happened that a bloody Avar between
the Batta-hattas and another tribe had just been
concluded, and the Ba'tas, haAnng been aTc*
torious, had in their posses si m many prisoners.
“ Long pig,” therefore, Avas plentiful and we
were consequently icspited. We Avould keep—
and improA'e.
The selfish savages fed us avcll, and we spoilt an
indolent time, A'aried by false alarms of approach-
ing feasts. They were genial fellows in their way
and never omitted enquiring after oaar health every
morning.
One day Richardson came to me much per-
turbed. “ This is too bad,” he said. “You know
Suchamucha, the chiefs sister? Well, she has
fallen in love Avith me and insists upon my marry-
ing her.”
“ So much the better for you,” I replied ; “ they
surely Avon’t dine off a relation.”
“There’s something in that,” he remarked
meditatively; “but if it ever came to Mrs. Mac-
pherson’s ears !’’
“Pooh, hQAv could it?” I returned— “don’t
throw away such a chance. I happen to know
that all the prisoners of war are finished, and our
guardians Rive been casting greedy glances at
both of us lately. Worse than all, AA'e’re getting
fat !"
[?]
After hesitating a few days longer he succumbed
to circumstances, and the marriage Avas cele-
brated, it being stipulated on his part that both
our lives should be spared. Thus did Richardson
settle down to, Suchamucha and domestic bliss !
Id c a fortnight had elapsed a vessel hoA'e in
eight and sent a boat ashore to trade. Naturally,
everybody was on. the alert avc Avere anxious to
escape, while the natives were suspicious of our
intentions and determined to keep us for their
future gratification..
Just how the trouble commenced I don’t know.
C think the Batta-battas Avantcd to annex tho
ooat and all its conte its, so the mate opened fire.
Phis, of course, somcAvhat distracted the attention
of our gua~ds, and avc made a sudden bolt, Avith
Such unueba shrieking at our heels. As avc
pelted along an idea flashed through my brain. I
acted instaitly upon it: dropped suddenly right
<n front of Richardson Over he went headlong,
and Suchamucha, avlio Avas at least as strong as
any ordinary man, fell on top of and clutched
him. I reached the sea a mid a shower of badly-
aimed missiles and was ha iled into the boat half-
drowned.
“How about tin other fellow? 1 ’ queried the
mate, who was making things lively with a
Snider,
“He’s all safe,’ I paatcl, “married chief’s
sister—pull like blazes—war-canoes coming!”
Of course, there wore no war-caioes, but I
throw that falsehood infer the usual spa.'e tear
of tho Recording Angel. The more hint of a
savage flotilla was enough, and, in a few minutes,
we we c safe aboard tho schooner. The last I
saw of the island was a dusky crowd headed by
Suchamucha, carrying up the struggling, kicking
form of my bated rival.
The first thing 1 did when T got back to Sydney
was to see the widow a id tell her of the fate of
her afllanced-
Now% here some
people say I did
wrong, but I acted
for the best. That is,
for my best.
Did T tell Mrs Mac.
that only a matter of
life and death had led
Richardson to go
through barbaric
man iage - rites with
an amazonian crea-
ture, six feet high,
with ears nearly
touching her shoul-
ders, a nose artifici-
ally flattened out,
and a mouth with two
rows of tiled teeth
i aside ?
No, I described her
as a tawny maiden
with fawn-like eyes
and rounded limbs,
her sinuous figure
clothed mostly in a
garland of brilliant
tropical flowers
(Snchamucha never
Avore anything to
speak of). I re-christ-
cncd her Sooshomaha
and said that Rich-
ardson always called
her “Sooshoo. ” I
told the widow that
the marriage cere-
mony was performed
by the local mission-
ary—(only one mis-
sionary ever landed
on Sagarnodu and he
gave them all indigestion)—and I Avound up
with a glowing picture of Richardson reposing
in a shady groA'e with his dusky bride; she
alteir.atc’y singing her native love-songs an 1
feeding him Avith bananas and bread-fruit. Before
this time I had never known that I was a poet.
Mrs. Macpherson fainted three times during the
recital, but insisted on my going through with it,
even to repeating the most amorous parts of the
narrative. Especially, being herself of bony and
angular architecture, did she linger upon the
A'olu ptnous curves of Sooshoo’s figure ; and how
she did grind her tooth at every fresh description
of the island beauty’s charms !
My AA'ay Avas now clear before me, and I urged
my suit with pitying, yet passionate, persistence.
I soon regained my lost ground, and in a month
or two Mrs. Macpherson and I were engaged.
I Avas A'isiting tho fair Avidow one afternoon.
In the midst of a tender scene the door-boll rang.
Wo heard a male voice parleying troublously wit h
the servant., we heard male footsteps dash past
her into the hall, wc seemed to feel a vigorous
and quick stride come right to the portal of our
retreat. Then, the door avus dashed open, and in
Avalkcd —Richardson ! Tho man T thought safe
for ever at Sagarnodu ! Ho had been rescued by
another vessel.
As Avas but natural under the circumstances,
Mrs. Mac. fainted away, and the detested Richard-
son andl wore immediately locked in deadly combat,
heedless, in our rage, both of hcrinscnsiblccomlilion
and her bric-it-brac. By the time that we had
lost our “first wind” and not recovered our
second, the widow revived, and a lively scene
commenced. First, she enquired after Mrs.
Richardson, and trusted that her complexion would
not suffer in the Sydney climate. Then she
Stated, still in a coldly sarcastic tone, that she
had heard that Mrs. R. had exquisite taste in
dress, and she must find out the name of her
dressmaker. She Was going on to ask if there
were yet any little Richardsons, When hysterics
overtook her, and the servants came to the rescue.
Richardson and I were forced to depart for the
time, which Wc did with mutually defiant scowls.
By what magic he regained his sway over the
widow I know not, but when next I presented
myself at Mrs, Macpherson’s abode she was “ not
at home,” although I distinctly saw her and
Richardson sitting together at tho Window.
Soon afterwards, a stroke of good luck hap-
pened to me—some mining shares 1 held suddenly
boomed in the market, and I made a very tidy
sum by their sale. I did not now so much regret
the loss of the widow, who, apart from her money,
Was an old vinegar- cruet, but I longed for
I WAS HAULED INTO THE BOAT HALF DROWSED.
revenge. When I heard of Richardson’s renewed
engagement I left Sydney.
The coquettish widow would, fora long wh'le
not consent to name the day ; she still professed to
believe there was something in that island story,
although Richardson swore it was only the base
and cowardly fabrication of an enemy who had
fled and left him there to be eaten. At last she
was won over, and a coming fashionable marriage
began to be talkep of in the society columns of
the press. The day arrived, and so did I.
The church was crowded, and when the clergy-
man (there were three of them) demanded
whether any person knew of any just cause or
impediment, &c., a tall, burly form in woman’s
clothes arose, and, in a harsh voice, remarked:
“Me, Misscc Richcrsen.” The faiming and con-
fusion amongst the women was awful when she
threw back her veil and displayed the grinning
cannibal countenance of a Sagarnodu savage, that
looked tho more grotesque because of the civilised
gown she wore. Most of the men thought it was
a very bal joke, but when I also arose and stated
that there were reasons which led me to believe
that the lady who had spoken merely asserted her
just rights, there was a dead silence, and all the
fainting women recovered. The officiating clergy-
man requested our presence in the vestry.
At the risk of my life I had returned to Saga-
modn and succeeded in kidnapping Suchamucku.
On the trip I had instructed her how to play her
part at a given signal, ami repeal the few words
I had taught her by rote. I am really convinced
that the poor heathen had conceived a strong and
lasting affection for Richardson, although people
refuse to believe it was pity that instigated me in
what I did ; they assort that it was a mean
revenge.
# # #
But sc 3 how poor a revenge it was! The
clergyman refused to proceed with the ceremony
until counsel’s opinion had been tikcn as to the
possible legality of any marriage-rite performed
amonght savages; and two days afterwards
Richardson, unannounced, burst into my apart-
ment, took me by the hand, and called me his
friend and saviour! “Man,” said he, “ 1 know
you meant to do me an ill-turn, but you’ve done
me a good one. Only for you I should now be
tied for life to that old virago who has nothing but
a life-interest in a little property left by her late
husband, and is head over ears in debt. Why, as
soon as the news got about that the wedding was
‘ off,” there wore half-a-dozen executions issued
and she has only been staving them off by saying
that she was going to marry a rich island trader.
Lot us have a drink.”
I have left off trying to get level with Richard^
son.
BINGHI.
REMINDED OF HIS DEAD MOTHER.
[EXTRACT FROM A PRIVATE LETTER.]
It was the latter part of August, 1891. A friend
and I had come down from Ramsgate to Mimter,
to see the venerable church there, which is a
thousand years old. I entered the churchyard
and seated myself upon a nameless grave while he
went in search of somebody to unlock the doors of
the edifice and show its wonders. In a few
minutes he returned in company with an elderly
lady, to whom he introduced mo, saying she was
the custodian and guide of the place. I gazed at
her face for some moments without a word. If
my own mother, dead and gone 15 years, had
come back to speak to her only son, I should
scarcely have been more astonished. For this
woman was almost my mother’s double ; the same
size, the same face, and the same way of parting
the hair and combing it in smooth bands from the
forehead. I told her so, and we were friends
before either fairly knew the other’s name. What
a queer world it is.
She then conducted us through the ancient
fane, and spoke of the long vanished past, of the
monks and nuns who onoe sang and prayed with-
in its walls, of the quaint carvings on the hard
oak seats in the chancel, of that precious relic, the
Cranmer Bible, which reposes in a glass box
against a pillar, and of many matters besides,
drawn from the apparently exhaustless well of her
detailed and accurate information.
Finally the talk veered round to the wholesome-
ness of the vicinity, the bracing nature of its sea
breezes, and so on. Then our guide, Mrs. Sarah
Herd, said !—“ I have lived here in Minster 50
years, and seen many ups and downs. One of my
sons is now in America, where he is doing well.
He wants me to leave England and make my home
with him, but I doubt if I ever shall. lam some-
what like that old yew-tree out in the yard, deeply
rooted to this soil, and might be the worse for
pulling up. Then lam getting on in life, and ills
grow apace with age. In the spring of 1878 I had
a serious attack. At first I lspew what
to make of it. There was no disease that I recog-
nised in particular. I felt tired in body and
weary m mind. There was nnffch pain lit my
chest and back, and a kind of tigliHHss at the
sides, as though physical force were applied there
to restrain me from moving. My appetite, which
was usually good, fell away ; and whatever I ate
or drank gave me pain, and I lived almost entirely
on bread and water. I was always in pain and
couldn’t sleep so as to feel refreshed by it. After
a time I grew so weak as to be unable to go about
my work. A bitter and sickening fluid arose into
my month, and I perspired to such an extent that
the sweat sometimes rolled off my face to the
door.”
I (the writer) break in upon Mrs. Herd’s story
at this point merely to say that this tendency to
sweat without the provocation of labour or of
exercise is always a sign of a debilitated condition
of the system.
It means that the blood is impure and im-
poverished, the kidneys working badly, and that
the body lacks nourishment and is living feebly on
what was previously stored in it. In other words,
the stomach lias refused its duty and the other
organs are in sympathy with it. Now wc will let
the lady proceed, begging pardon for the inter-
ruption.
She went on to say;—“For a time I tried to
cure myself with various domestic remedies which
sometimes answer. But they failed, and I con-
sulted a physician. With all respect to the doctors,
they occasionally fail too. This one did. You
know there comes a time in all long illnesses when
we get in some way used to pain and misery, and
make no further efforts to get rid of it. In fact,
we don’t know how, and so don’t try. For about
three years I remained wretched and ailing, and
dull unhappy years they were. My sufferings
were beyond all I had ever known before, yet
there seemed nothing to do but to bear them as
patiently as I could. At this date, 1881, certain
friends of mine spoke to me of the great benefit
they had received from the use of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup, for indigestion and dyspepsia. This threw
light on my mind, although I cannot say it made
me at once a believer in Seigel’s Syrup. At
length, however, in July 1881, I began to take it.
In all I used six bottles, and found my health
fully restored. Ten years have elapsed, and I
have had no attack since. But if Ido in future I
shall know where to put my hand on the remedy.”
Our visit being virtually over, wo called fora
few moments at Mrs. Herd’s home, 2, High-street,
Minster, Kent, and then wended our way hack to
Ramsgate.
Niw York, October, 1891.
C.M.R.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, rvith whom ive do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
i Correspondence " column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, arc specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the a dress is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we de-ire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY. MARCH 4, 1893.
The Unfinished Policy.
The present state of things offers to the Third
Party in New South Wales politics such a
chance as it has never enjoyed since the first
few weeks of its existence. For the time being
it absolutely holds the balance of power, and
as it is impossible to prophesy how it will stand
after next general election it must either avail
itself of the opportunity or passively look on
awaiting the extinction which is inevitable
should it return to face the electors empty-
handed. Most of the important Government
measures which are now water-logged on the
business paper are measures of which the
democratic party as a whole cordially
approves, and the one disadvantage connected
with them is that neither the Government, nor
the leaders of the Opposition, nor the two
weary, dejected and demoralised parties which
make up the bulk of the Assembly, seem
capable or desirous of pushing any one of them
to a satisfactory conclusion. The opportunity of
the Labour-party is, therefore, tosendin its united
ultimatum that this year, at any rate, there will
be no unfinished business, and that it will sup-
port the Dibbs Government against its will—
for the Government has apparently no serious
desire for any support which will force it to
carry out its own democratic programme to
the bitter end—until every measure of import-
ance on the business paper is either made law
or lost in the Upper House. In other words,
it should announce definitely that the Assembly
is going to sit until every item is disposed of,
even though it should plod on its weary, mill-
horse round till the Christmas of 1893, and that
so long as the Dibbs Cabinet is prepared to
drag its exhausted followers every night to
the House of Parliament and plough along
with its mass of promised legislation, the
Labour members will give it their unani-
mous support. But if any attempt is made
to close the session and leave even one
democratic measure unfinished, the Labour vote
goes unanimously with the Opposition, and the
Dibbs Cabinet becomes deceased there and
then. The Third Party is fully strong enough
to take up such a position if, for once, and once
only, it will sink all internal differences, drop
all external sympathies, and devote itself
strictly to business. 4
# * *
Such a proceeding would be a picturesque
vengeance on the old politicians of the Assem-
bly. The Dibbs Government could not, with-
out convicting itself of unutterable hypocrisy,
quarrel with a party which came to its rescue
in the hour of need with only one resolution —
to die at its post if necessary, and to make
others do the same, for the sake of carrying out
the Government’s own programme. 'The Cabinet,
in all probability, would never have introduced
that programme if it had foreseen any such in-
convenient enthusiasm, but it could not do
otherwise than accept the situation. To all
the miscellaneous obstructionists of the House
the movement would come as a revelation. It
would be a new departure in politics. Already
this session fully four months have been spent
in obstruction, sometimes by the Opposition,
sometimes by the Government itself ; but when
it became evident that by its own tactics the
stonewall party had only doomed itself to four
or six, or if necessary, twelve months
more labour to make up for lost time
the joy of talking against futurity would
considerably fade away. The advent of a
party which is firmly resolved that every demo-
cratic measure already introduced shall have a
fair chance and an impartial hearing, even if
Reid and Parkes should die of exhaustion upon
the floor of the House, and which, if baulked in
this endeavour, will promptly wipe out the
Cabinet, would be a most awful awakening.
Even the largest and noisiest politician would
not desire to meet more than once with
such an experience. The deceased Premier
Atkinson, of Maoriland, habitually followed
this system, and did good service for the Con-
servative party thereby. There was no un-
finished business in the days of the Continuous
Ministry, unless Atkinson so desired for his
own purposes. Also, there never was any
obstruction after the first month of the session.
Obstruction only meant 24-hours’ sittings, and
sessions that lasted nine months or more out of
the twelve, and when it became known that
the old soldier meant to go on to the day of
resurrection rather than leave any of his mea-
sures incomplete, his opponents invariably
collapsed.
# * •
The present is the time above all others when
the New South Wales Labour-party should
adopt Atkinson’s tactics. The Dibbs Govern-
ment has committed itself to a number of
valuable democratic measures, and if it is
allowed to peacefully drop them overboard at
the end of the session, there is no possible
guarantee that it may ever so commit itself
again. The future is full of possibilities —
mostly bad ones. By next session a Coalition
may have arisen, and the parties to the unholy
compact may be strong enough to go in for un-
disguised Conservatism after the fashion
of 'the Grifeilwraith league and covenant
and J. B. Patterson and Co. Or the
Government may have sold itself and its
party to the National Ass ; or Reid and Bur-
dekin may be running a Cabinet of lawyers
and landlords, or any number of other mis-
fortunes may eventuate. But, for the moment,
the Ministry chooses to pose as a democratic
organisation, and the business of the demo-
cratic party is to keep it at that. The Local
Government Bill alone is worth securing at the
cost of a twelve-months’sitting, but much more
than that can be gained by a party which holds
the balance of power, and which announces its
fixed resolve to camp in the House of Parlia-
ment, if necessary, till the world grows old,
rather than let any single one of the Govern-
ment’s promises go with the unfinished business
of 1893.
Compensation to Publicans.
One of the great political contests of the imme-
diate future in Australia is that between the
liquor and the anti-liquor interests, and the
signs are that this will shortly become the
leading question of the day. It promises to be
“ a triangular duel,” the three parties engaged
being that particular section of the community
which lives by alcohol, and which conse-
quently desires to extend the use of that
fluid as much as possible ; the solemn, black-
coated, black-hatted faction which wants to
utterly abolish intoxicants, and send into
penal servitude—or thereabouts —every one
making, buying, selling, using, or being in
possession of the unclean thing ; and, lastly, the
local-option party (much of it really prohibi-
tionist at heart) which recognises it as a prin-
ciple of the Local Government policy that every
community shall have the right to restrict or
abolish the sale of liquor in its own district as
it may see fit, by decision of the majority. Of
these, the latter party is much the most power-
ful, and in all probability the local option
system will be adopted in all parts of Australia
at an early date. This system, in fact, is
already so “far recognised that the struggle
hardly concerns it in any way. The real ques-
tion at issue is that of compensation to those
publicans who are to be dispossessed of their
licenses and driven out of business by
the popular vote. It is universally assumed
that the immediate result of local option will
be a great reduction in the number of publicans’
licenses—the idea of utterly abolishing alcohol
by Act of Parliament being confined to the
shrill fanatics already mentioned ; and, there-
fore, the compensation question is practically
the only one left for discussion.
* * *
There is a strong case to be made out both for
and against compensation. That advanced on
the affirmative side, however, can be put in
much the smaller compass. The law, ever since
there was a law, has recognised the publican’s
business as a legitimate calling. It has expressly
licensed him to carry on that calling, and has
made him pay for the privilege of doing so.
Although Ids licence is only granted for one
year, and the State is therefore at full liberty
to cancel it without compensation at the end of
that period, yet it has always been understood
that it would be renewed indefinitely, subject
to the good behaviour of the holder. For
instance, there is in Sydney one publican who
has personally had his licence renewed for 50 con-
secutive years. That such an understanding exists,
the palatial hotels of Melbourne, Sydney, and
other cities are sufficient evidence ; no sane
speculator would invest large sums of money
in erecting a huge structure which would
outlive its usefulness in the space of twelve
months, and but for this implied contract
with the State the liquor business would
be conducted almost exclusively in tents and
wigwams. If the State has decided to change
its policy then it should do so at its own
expense —not by ruining a particular class and
confiscating its means of livelihood. The resolve
to reform at somebody else’s cost is a cheap
spasm of virtue at the best, and is more closely
related to burglary than to piety ; the desire,
as manifested by the extreme teetotal party, to
reform solely at its enemy’s cost, is a cheaper
virtue still, and is near akin to that patriotism
which consists in sacrificing the old man on the
altar of his country, and then inheriting his
estate as the result of his death. T his is the
case for the party which advocates compensa-
tion, and, though not absolutely convincing, it
is undeniably a strong one.
# # *
There is also much, as the Sydney Morning
Herald would remark, to be said upon theother
side. In the first place, no one, save a few
hopeless and unconsidered fanatics, anticipates
the utter abolition of public-houses ; it is only
a reduction of their number that is contem-
plated by the great majority of electors. I lie
case will be one of the survival of the fittest.
The kind of hotel which will be most promptly
suppressed is the low-grade city drinking shop,
which is one-half house of ill-fame and one-half
rendezvous for bullies, larrikins and tenth-rate
bruisers —the dirty little place which is ap-
proached by falling down a worn and dirty
step, where the counter is sticky with ancient
heel-taps, and there are defunct flies in the black
and muddy “ colonial beer,” and where a fat
and frowsy barmaid and a pimpled landlord
loom up against a cheerless vista of neglected
bottles in the background. And in the country
districts the bush “shanty” will go —the
establishment where there are cakes of
nefarious tobacco at the bottom of the
rum - cask, and the loud - mouthed robber
behind the counter lives by dispensing
thinly-disguised kerosene, blue ruin, and sudden
death. The individuals who run these establish-
ments will be abolished principally because of
their foulness, and if they are compensated by
the State they will, practically, be paid for
being foul. There are so many of their class
that the local option system might be in force
for years without extinguishing a single hotel-
keeper who was not more in need of six months
hard labour than of a vote out of the national
Treasury, and, viewed in this light, compensa-
tion looks like a premium for vice, and a reck-
less waste of public money.
# * *
Moreover, if the publican be compensated that
will not be the end, but only the beginning of
the difficulty. There are infinite possibilities
that lie beyond. \\ hen the nation decides to
recognise the liquor-seller’s claim then it may
also be called upon to recognise the claims of
the brewer and distiller, the importer of
foreign liquors, the merchant, his clerks and
commercial travellers, the licensed victualler,
the vigneron and his employes, the vigneron s
landlord—if he has one—the bottler, the cooper,
the man who prints the labels, the farmer who
suffers through the decreased demand for hops,
malt, &c., "the publican’s landlord —whose
premises are enormously diminished in value
through the withdrawal of the licence, and
many others directly or indirectly connected
with the trade. Moreover, though the poor,
down-trodden publican is invariably put for-
ward as the raison d'etre of the compensa-
tion principle, yet probably 90 per cent, of
him will get none of the compensation in any
case. He is a mere stalking-horse in the
matter, and those who wail most loudly about
his woes fully intend to collect the money out
of the Treasury themselves and leave him
penniless. The small publican, in a very large
proportion of cases, is a mere agent of the
brewer or the merchant. He is squeezed and
rack-rented by them ; when they are satisfied
he has so little left for himself that he has
hardly anything to be compensated for ; and if
the State pays him for his loss, or alleged loss,
through the closure of his house, the money will
really go to the brewer, while the ostensible
recipient is altogether ignored. And the brewer,
as it happens, is an individual who needs com-
pensation less than the publican, for if half the
existing establishments are closed, the business
at those which remain open will be so materially
increased that his loss will in reality be much
less than it appears.
It has undoubtedly been a common assump-
tion that a license is something more than a
mere permit, to ssll intoxicants for a period of
twelve months, and that the State recognised
the moral right of the publican to perpetual
renewals so long as his house was well-con-
ducted and kept in reasonably good order and
repair. On the strength of this assumption
large sums have often been paid for the purchase
of licences, they being regarded by both buyer
and seller as a permanent property. But on the
other hand the State has never done anything to
endorse this idea. It has merely issued an
annual licence, and beyond the four corners of
that document it is responsible for nobody’s
assumptions. The men who conducted “con-
sultations ” also assumed, because the law took
no notice of them for many years, that their
industry was permanent, but when it was sum-
marily abolished in Maoriland and Victoria
nobody suggested that the “ sweep ’’-promoters
should receive a State grant to make up for the
loss of their livelihood. It has been under-
stood, by old English and Australian custom,
that the landowner is a privileged individual
and practically exempt from taxation. He has
also assumed that his immunity would last
for ever, as the enormous prices paid
for city lands sufficiently demonstrate ;
nevertheless the demand for a ponderous land-
tax is steadily growing, and when that tax is
imposed those holders of city allotments, who
bought at boom prices, will occupy a position
almost as bad as the dispossessed publican. Nct
they obviously cannot be compensated. The
Dibbs Government in New South Wales has
"ranted a certain measure of Protection to cer-
tain industries, and capital has been invested in
some of them on the assumption —backed up by
the assurance of a laree Parliamentary majority
_that this Protection will be permanent. If
the Foreign-trade party again secures otiice,
however, it will promptly and wilfully dem y
these new industries and cause the h ° ls 1
the capital invested in them, ail q SS
the idea of paying compensation to
capitalists or their employes would he rec ■, le
with derision.' The position of the nuhT'
may also be very much akin to that of the ICaU
who conducts a noxious trade iu a ln ( ail
When the public awakes to a better r,
ledge of the laws of liealth and fully real°"
the material stench which that trade creates*
simply orders —by legal enactment— it s ’ /
moval, and so far from compensating
facturer, it generally fines him instead. \m Uu '
the public fully awakens to the moral stench' 6 *
the liquor business it will probably do SOl 01
thing of the same kind, and with just as un"T
reason. c 1
* * *
All the same, The Bulletin does not asse
that the dispossessed publican should be refu« i
compensation. It holds, however, that 1
should be compensated more as a matter "f
expediency than of cast-iron justice, and sole!
at the expense of those who profit direct/
by his abolition, and that it is hnpossibl
to make out any case whereby this charge
be levied upon the nation at large. The q Ues
tion at present is entirely one concerning the
reduction of the number of public-houses—their
entire abolition is a matter that is lost in the
very remote future. The reduction in the
number of public-houses and the reduction in
the national drink bill will certainly not pro-
ceed upon the same ratio. If one-half the
hotels throughout Australia are closed, the pro-
babilities are that—through the greater concen-
tration of trade, and the saving in rent and
working expenses—the profits of the other half
will be increased 100 per cent., and this result
will be arrived at even though the consumption
of drink is largely diminished. Therefore, the
simplest course is to let the liquor-traffic pay for
its own gradual diminution —or abolition—by
largely increasing licence-fees, and thereby
creating a fund, out of which hona-Jide publicans
—not brewers’ dummies —and their employes—
which latter class seems to have been entirely
overlooked —can be compensated for the loss of
their employment, they being the most con-
spicuous sufferers. And then, if the abstinence
movement gains ground, the process can be
continued until there is only one publican left,
and he is the State ; and then, presumably,
there will be a Minister for Liquor, and the
barmen’s billets in the great national pub, will
serve as a refuge and reward for ex-Premiers
and other deserving politicians. And, last of
all, this place too may perhaps be closed, with-
out compensation, for the State will be the
loser, and there will be no person on whom to
shift the liability. And after that the com-
pensation question will arise for the last time,
for the teetotal lecturer will cry with a loud
voice and want to know who is going to com-
pensate him for the loss of his occupation and
his salary, now that there is no more use for
him in the land, and there will be no answer.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Justice of Labour Representation.
Says Walter Back hot :
A great many ideas, a great many feelings have gathered
among artisans—a peculiar intellectual life has sprung up
among then). They believe that they have interests which
are misconceived or neglected ; that they know something
which others do not know ; that the thoughts of Parlia-
ment are not as their thoughts. They ought to be allowed
to try to convince Parliament; their notions ought to be
stated as those of other classes are stated ; their advocates
should be heard as other people’s advocates are heard. .
. . . As long as a great class, congregated in political
localities and known to have political thoughts and
wishes, is without notorious and palpable advocates in
Parliament, we may prove on paper that our representa-
tion is adequate, but the world will not believe it. . • ■
It is in vain to demonstrate that the working classes have
no grievances: that the middle classes have done all
possible for them, and so on, with a crowd of arguments
which I need not repeat, for the newspapers keep them
in type, and we know them by heart. But so long as the
“ gross appearance ” is that there are no evident, inces-
sant representatives to speak the wants of artisans, the
“ great reality ” will be a diffused dissatisfaction. Every-
body must say now, “ our representative system must be
imperfect, for an immense class has no members to speaK
tor it.” . . . The way to stop the complaint that
artisans have no members is to give them members-to
create a body of representatives, chosen by artisans,
believing, as Mr. Carlyle would say, “that artisamsm is
the one thing needful.”

Wales to resist all attempts to “ strengthen -
as Parkes truly, but unwisely for the suc-

—makes advanced legislation possible. J Ol

The “ special correspondent ” of a London
paper gives a brief summary of what Freetrade
has done for the agricultural interest in Eng-
land
Dealiiw with Suffolk, which is not the worst of the
shires, from 50 to 75 per cent, of the county seats are
deserted by the old families and let to brokers, manufac-
turers, and tradesmen from I-ondon, who keep the land
in cultivation at a loss simply for their pleasure. The
case is quoted of a tenant, farmer who, twenty years ago,
purchased his farm for £9OOO, and who has lately had to
nart with it for £2OOO. Another estate is mentioned
which was worth £12,000 a short time asm,
and which can now be purchased for £2OOO. In
Essex recently a two hundred-acre farm, within
carting distance of London, was sold for £S per acre.
Thousands of acres are now being cultivated by the land-
lords, through lack of tenants. An experienced and care-
ful farmer from mid Suffolk expressed the opinion to the
correspondent that two more years of such disaster would
exhaust the resources of the lauded interest, and, in fact,
“witness the end of tenant-farming.” An agriculturist
of standing in the county of Suffolk submitted to the
correspondent the average balance sheet of a farm of 2138
acres for six years (had the figures for 1802 been taken, the
result would have been much worse). The receipts are
put down at £5661, and the expenditure at £5085, leaving
only £579 for the landlord and tenant between them.
The cents have fallen all over tire country from 40 to 50
percent. A farm of 400 acres is instanced, upon which
the owner since 1880 lias spent £2OOO in farm buildings
and improvements, and up to 1880 it was let at £2 6s. 2d.
per acre. The landlord is now glad to take 16s. lid. A
third case is mentioned of a f inn, which at one time com-
manded 255. per ao.e ; it is now let at 6s. The truth is,
states the correspondent, “that in the county of Suffolk
anybody can have land by merely paying the tithes and
charges.”
The Bulletin would like to know how many
of the 52 signatories of that exceedingly
illogical and illiterate letter “to Mr. J. (J.
Neild, as Chairman of the Select Committee
on the N.S. Wales Vice Suppression Bill,” were
Womanhood Suffragists and Woman’s Rightsers
and pledged to advocate absolute equality with
man. And, gracious goodness, how many
belong to the N.S. Wales Women’s Literary
Society ? Here’s a taste
We would respectfully point out to you that the law
enacts that young girls may not be apprenticed to a trade
without consent of parent or guardian till they are 21,
that girls cannot he married without the same consent,
nor are they allowed the management of property until
they are the same age ; and we would urge that a State
which requires want of experience and development in
(jins to that age, and the necessity of protecting them
under these circumstances till they are able to decide for
tlumselves, should see that it is fitting to protect them
up to the far less develoued age of 17 from the risk of in-
curring the most terrible evil that can befall a young girl
—the loss of her honour—an evil far worse than that of
suicide, from which the State endeavours to protect both
inen ana women to any age.
Adams on Davitt.
Dear Bulletin, Francis Adams’ rancorous
attack on Davitt in a recent issue, is grossly char-
ac.enscd by the writer’s propensity for the use of
vitriolic! epithets. I know nothing of Mr. Adams
personally. But Ido know the man to whom he
aies apply the terms—liar, charlatan, cheat !
purely the writer never could have carefully and
deliberately selected them. If it can be shown
en by the act of proof it is shown
i. ■,, * knows nothing whatever about
t- . the attack has a middle-class savour, and
inirlriia f ams f reedom I enquire—ls Adams a
f \ not, the aroma of middle-
rp i at;re d of Davitt with which the whole attack
bp f coincidence. “ You are going to
camp „PP ed back to the gutter, out of which you
Pamoli Sa^ S j^ ams t° Davitt. The fact that
a somewhat similar phrase to
drew frp lS rf 0 - a coincidence ; and that phrase
Sr the burnt, ’which has a bitter
Cendant- If m dre J a !l d ’ that Parnell was the des-
laudatirm rTi Cromwell’s swashbucklers. A
Klasspd nv°i!m 16 renfi ß a de Chamberlain, the eye-
been frnm o' *d-scented, ex-radical, might also have
pathies K fcl p epeitof a ma n of middle-class sym-
pathies ; but never from a true democrat.
* * #
P.wip'd'"';. n ° t° involve a denunciation of
the lat-to.. . a ., d cfiance of Davitt; but Adams says
defendVho r - Parnell, and it is necessary to
ch arge a L any rate > from such a strong
responsible hoso wh o malign the living
dead Th Q u t 10 . c °nsequences, if any, to the
nell“wit-L charge is that Davitt turned on Par-
bed.” Ti; a v - sava F e ferocity because Parnell had
■word nf Up llston cal, now, that Parnell gave his
unstained f™° Ur J lO davitt that he would come
thestint^^u* 6 ° rd ? al of trial; and that on
in America ,°,i ! al; Davitt called upon his friends
to the Inai - Australia to continue their trust
the resell , t . heir Parliamentary leader. Well,
Davitt , un< dcfended trial proved that
as Adams .« gr ? S ? y deceived. Knowing the fact,
bulk of the T k - i UOW ’ as Davitt knew, that the
traihinc n 1 people are, by nature and
tnde towards i\ nd .unbending in their atti.
owaras th e laws of sexual morality,
Davitt endeavoured to prevent the continuance
of an impossible leadership of the cause for which
Davitt lived, worked, and suffered. The influence
of the “ordeal of battle ” is as strong now as it
was in the days of chivalry. It was shown in the
mad enthusiasm when Parnell triumphed over the>
Times. It was not less apparent in the mournful
silence whicli followed the Divorce Court verdict.
Where was the savage ferocity here? The “savage
ferocity ” came later, when Parnell’s hired ruffians,
in Kilkenny broke their sticks on Davitt’s body.
Adams cannot be ignorant of this.
* « »
There never was a rivalry between Parnell and
Davitt. Davitt had no desire for parliamentary
fame, and Parnell stood easily at the head of the
parliamentarians of his day. But there must have
been in the mind and heart of Parnell the cold
contempt which a descendant of a Cromwellian
trooper feels for the descendant of a peasant whose
soil the trooper took. The injurer never forgives.
And there must have been in Davitt’s heart a.
knowledge that from Parnell, victorious and all-
powerful in an Irish Parliament, little could be
expected for the peasant or the wage-earner. It
was only in the hour of his desperation that Par-
nell, the politician with nerves of chilled steel,
made his frantic appeal to the Labour bodies of
Ireland, for whom Davitt has given years of labour
and self-sacrifice. “Davitt left Parnell,” says
Adams. No ! Parnell caused the severance, and
upon him the onus rests.
* * *
“ Impostor !” says Adams to Davitt. This
needs no answer. If there is anything in the
world which Davitt is not, it is that. Adams has
taken the first epithet that sprung to his mind,
without examining its significance. “ Charlatan !”
says Adams. Davitt a charlatan ! One of the
truest friends of the people that ever lived ; who
has suffered and toiled more than anyone now
alive in the Irish movement, who toiled along in
obscurity, who overcame the handicap of the loss
of his rjght arm in a factory accident, and
educated himself in moments snatched from hours
of degrading toil. Charlatan ! The man who
was a staunch and tried revolutionist until con-
vinced that revolutionary success was impossible
—who suffered for his acts and opimons, not with
six months’ imprisonment in Kilmainham as a
political prisoner, but with years of immurement
and convict treatment in Portland prison, dragging
his leg-irons round with him. Had he been a Russian
and endured such treatment, Adams might have
reversed his epithets ; for it is a noted character-
istic of such English writers that they shriek for
the liberty for the Russian which they daily deny
to the Irishman. Had Davitt been of the social
rank of William O’Brien, society might have
agitated for his release, and Adams might have
withheld his epithets.
• # #
“ Cheat!” says Adams. Davitt a cheat! The
Irishman of all others trusted of the English
democracy, who, in Adams’ own words of admis-
sion, has “preached passionately to us all the
gospel of the unity of the English and the Irish
peoples.” But he did more. He preached the
solidarity of the cause of the English and Irish
proletariat; he showed the unlanded in England
that they were vitally interested in the struggle
of the unlanded of Ireland. And he is trusted
and loved to-day by the English labour democracy,
as anyone who has ever attended a great labour
meeting in London (which I have been privileged
to do) can testify. He fought and fights with his
pen and tongue for land-nationalisation. He
valued the good of his country and his class above
the maintenance of one picturesque, historical
figure. He is sick and weary of the inter-racial
struggle which is born of national prejudice ; and
he knows that, from his point of view, as opposed
to the middle-class Parnells and Redmonds, the
total separation of Ireland from England will-
in the future struggle of the poor and lowly of all
nations against economic conditions—only mean
another Government to fight. Can this man be a
cheat, or is his accuser one ?
* * *
The allusion to Davitt’s support of John Morley
in Newcastle is easily explained. John Morley
was opposed by Mark Hammond, a Conservative,
and his defeat would have meant a further blow
to the cause Davitt maintains. Does not Adams
know the sort of man Morley‘s opponent was as
well as I do ? If not, he must be blindly ignorant
of the labour movement on the Tyne.
* # *
To those who know Davitt, Adams’ words can
only excite disgust. Who can look at that face as
pictured even in the commonest oleograph without
seeing the volcanic force of the man who came up
from the masses to champion their cause—“ from
the gutter,” as Adams puts it —to the Himalaya
on which he now stands, notwithstanding his
calumniators ? Who, knowing the history of that
noble and unselfish life, could gaze without pity
upon the figure of the man, as I saw him, walking
through the snow in Fleet-street without over-
coat and with his empty sleeve looped up to his
shoulder, a slouch hat drawn over his eyes—those
wonderful, honest, coal-black eyes, which he and
John Burns resemble each other in possessing?
And who could fail to be touched by the mourn-
ful tone of his voice when he said (it was after
the Kilkenny election): “It was the first time I
was ever struck by an Irishman !” This man was
no charlatan, no impostor, no liar, and no thief.
# # *
It is not difficult to pierce through Adams’ thin
veneer of Socialism if he is judged by this article.
His admiration for Chamberlain, the apostate
from Radicalism, is enough. Why should
Chamberlain command the admiration of Adams ?
For the same sympathies which excite his loathing
of Davitt. I wonder? Chamberlain was driven into
his present position by his rivalry of Gladstone.
It is an open secret that he would have made
terms with the Irish party, but that the “ old
Parliamentary hand ” was too quick for him. He
still calls himself a “ Liberal.” W. S. Gilbert, in
his travesty of “Hamlet,” defines that unhappy
prince’s state of mind as “Idiotically sane with
lucid intervals of lunacy.” I can only describe
Chamberlain’s state of mind by paraphrasing
Gilbert. Chamberlain has had idiotically Liberal
moments, but his lucid intervals of Conservatism
are likely to be of longer duration.
* # *
I wish I were better qualified to defend this
true man, Davitt, from a cowardly assailant.
But Davitt himself will reply in good time if he
considers his assailant worthy of it. Is the moral
of this attack the deduction that to escape from
the vilification of even a Socialist one must either
be the possessor of the lands bequeathed by a
robber ancestor or the owner of a screw factory ?
“Back to the gutter from whence you came!” says
Adams to Davitt. When Adams and Davitt and
you, Mr. Editor, and I will have joined Parnell—
“ In the grave’s democracy,”
Davitt’s name will be enrolled with those of all
countries, and in all ages, who have loved the
people, fought for them, or suffered for them,
worked for them, or died for them. Davitb’s
deeds shall live long after oblivion has overtaken
every scurrilous scribbler who maligns nim.“
Yours truly,
John D. Fitzgerald.
Parliament House, Sydney. Feb.
[Francis Adams’ letter to The Bulletin was
signed by himself, and the comments therein were
printed simply as the opinions of a widely-known
and forceful writer. Nevertheless, we are very
sorry that we allowed the appearance of what is
clearly an unjust attack on Davitt. —Ed. D.]
The Victorian Mace Scandal.
A SYNOPSIS of leading evidence, as published in
Melbourne newspapers, given at the Missing Mace
Enquiry, .before the Vio. Parliamentary Board.
First Day—February 15.
The Serojeant-at-Anns of the Vie. Assembly said ; “ The
Speaker slept in the next room to mine (on the night
when, presumably, the mace was stolen). On that night
the House was locked up by the housekeeper. He locks
the House as soon as the Speaker and those staying here
have gone upstairs. I did not see Sir Matthew go up that
night—we did not all go upstairs together.
Housekeeper Pearce stated: “I don’t think that the
Speaker slept here that’ night. I forget how many slept
in the House that night.”
Second Day—February 16.
The Sergeant-at-Arms (recalled) said: “There was an
error in his evidence of the previous day. Sir M. H.
Davies had been in the habit of staying for a time after
the House rose, but he had ascertained that Sir Matthew
didn’t sleep there on the night in question.”
Housekeeper Pearce (re-called) said: “ Mr. Watson, the
doorkeeper, and Mr. Worthington were the only persons
who slept on the premises that night. (N.B.—The
Sergeant at-Arms is Mr. Upward.) A number of people
might have slept in the building that night without his
knowledge.”
John Brock, ex-M.L.A.: “ Denied that he had told
My ring, or anyone else, that the mace was at ‘Annie
Wilson’s.’ ”
Henry Hamilton, accountant, said that: “ Shortly after
the loss of the mace, Mr. Watt, one of the employes at
Parliament House, complained to him that the detectives
had been following him, and said that the}’ were a lot of
fools. Watt said that he knew where the mace
■was.”
Third Day—February 22,
Sir Matthew Davies said ; “ The rumours about his fre-
quently sleeping in the House were totally unfounded.
He had only slept there about three times altogether.”
Fife Duff Walt, doorkeeper: “ Denied having ever
made a statement that he knew where the mace was.”
Samuel My ring, cordial manufacturer at Benalla:
“ Deposed that Mr. Brock, late M.L.A., told him that the
mace had been taken in a drunken spree to the house of a ,
woman named Annie Wilson, and that he had seen it
there.”
Maurice Brodzky said: “On Saturday November 12, I
had a long conversation with a police officer on the sub-
ject. He volunteered the information that the mace had
been found, and that the police knew where it was. He
said, ‘ It is in the very same house where they were going
to lay the telephone on, which caused such a commotion.'
He mentioned several members of Parliament as being in
the habit of frequenting that house, and said that this was
why the evidence was smothered. He also said that the
information was known to every member of the force, and
was common talk. He told me that two women were in
the Speaker’s room with a member—wot Sir M. H. Davies
—and one of Hum had a long hood and took the mace out
in her arms. The police officer who gave me this infor-
mation was Detective-sergeant Lomax.
Fourth Day—February 23.
John Stevens, manager of the Mutual Store, “ stated
that he had been informed by a constable at the Licensing
Court, on December 1, that the mace had been taken to a
house of ill-fame. Ho volunteered the statement. I
asked him if it svas public property, and he replied that it
was not yet, but would shortly be.”
Alfred Lomax, sergeant of detective police, simply
denied that he had told Brodzky anything at all concern-
ing the mace. When asked by Brodzky—“ What has
become of the mace, and what is your opinion about it? ”
he (Lomax) had replied, “I don’t know. I haven’t
formed any opinion.” What Brodzky stated was abso-
lutely without any foundation at all—according to
Lomax.
Mr. Brodzky was not re-called. Many witnesses
were re-called in order to tone down their previous
evidence, but Mr. Brodzky, after being publicly
denounced as a liar, was not asked to come back
and repudiate the charge. Neither, by the way,
was Mr. Brock, who seems to have been called
early by mistake. Had Mr. Myring’s evidence
preceded that of Mr. Brock, the latter could have
airily disposed of Myring, just as Keys and Lomax
disposed of Hamilton and Brodzky. A careful
examination of the Missing Mace Enquiry dis-
closes the fact that every witness (excepting Mr.
Stephens) whose evidence tended in any way to
support newspaper information, either contra-
dicted a first statement, or was flatly accused
of perjury by some other witness. The de-
cision of the Board, it seems, will be com-
municated to the Governor in Council at as
early a date as possible. Meanwhile, the Board
must make up its mind to separate the lambs
from the liars, and to discover good and sufficient
cause for declaring that some of the lambs are not
liars, and vice-versa. In the all-important cases of
Myring and Brodzky the Board will doubtless
consider carefully whether they, or the parties
contradicting their respective statements, had the
more reason for falling into error or committing
black perjury. But before the Governor in
Council has received this almighty decision, he
will probably have heard the public’s decision on
hectoring Charles Taylor, and the make-weights
who supported him in this lamentable failure to
purge the mace-disappearance scandal.
The chairman at ex-Treasurer M‘Millan’s
meeting: “ I am here this evening because I am
an Australian.” If Mr. M‘Lellan, whoever he
is, will think hard he will discover that he was
there because the other man also was named Mac.
While in Northern India, M'Hwraith is
said to have made satisfactory arrangements with
the Indian Government for the importation to
Queensland of regular supplies of coolies. The
traffic will be supervised by an officer resident in
Brisbane, called the Protector of Indian Immi-
grants. He will be paid by the Q. Government,
but will be accountable to the Indian Government
alone. The agreement-term of service for coolies
is to be five years, during which they will be
engaged exclusively in tropical agriculture. They
may then elect to be returned to India, or to settle
permanently. If they choose to remain, they will
be under no especial industrial disabilities, but
may engage in any trade. By this back-door, the
pastoralists will ultimately get the coolie labour
they have long sighed for.
A Bulletin correspondent points out that
Professor Anderson-Stuart now gets .£lO4O salary
as Professor of Physiology, at Sydney University,
besides an allowance for house-rent; also, £lOO a
year as Government medical adviser, and .£630 as
health and immigration officer, in which latter
capacities he has a free railway-pass, and dra.ws
two guineas a day from time to time for travelling
expenses. A. few years ago he held the dual office of
Professor of Anatomy and Physiology, and it is
understood that he was relieved of the anatomical
chair on the ground that one man could not
reasonably he expected to perform both sets of
duties. In view of this, his present Pooh-bah-ish
position is interesting. Our correspondent
suggests that there should be laid before Parlia-
ment a list of the other State medical appoint-
ments with their salaries, allowances, perquisites,
and privileges.

at this office insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must in every case appear on MS.

“ Niobe,” which stony female of ancient
Greece is running this week at Sydney Criterion,
is, in its beginnings, practically “ Pygmalion and
Galatea” brought up to date. Like Galatea,
Niobe awakes unexpectedly and finds herself
somewhat out of keeping with her surroundings,
but after that the resemblance becomes indistinct.
Galatea came to life in her native Athens, or
thereabouts, while Niobe, who went to sleep some
time when the first Peloponnesian war was in full
blast, resurrects in the house of Peter Dunn, of
London, at a moment when all the family is out;
and Mr. Dunn, with a strange, ancient Greek lady
on hand, and his female relatives knocking at the
door, wildly passes her off as the new governess.
Regarded as a goveruets, Niobe is a wondrous
comedy, but an educational failure. She sug-
gests that Dunn s eldest and most haggard and
objectionable maiden aunt should be sold as a
slave; she wants to have another objectionable
person ostracised, and she addresses the big, con-
ventional slavey as “Helot.” Also, she implores
the gods to drop a thunderbolt or an eight-day
clock or something on the head of somebody else ;
and when the real governess turns up and wants
her Sunday frock, which Niobe is wearing on her
stately form, the classic lady gets upon the house-
roof and appeals to the deities of Olympus to
utterly blast and consume the said governess, and
keel-haul her unquiet ghost, so that nothing might
remain of her but tap
boots and ashes ; and all
the butchers’ boys in the
neighbourhood gather to
listen to the malediction,
and give a hundred and
sixteen cheers. Also,
Niobe desires to hire
a slave to murder
Cecil Ward, and she
suffers from a fixed
conviction that every-
body’s name is Hippar-
chus, or words to that
effect, and that Dunn
has power to put all
his relatives up to auc-
tion. Niobe is a bril-
liantly mirthful person-
age while she lasts, and
at the end she declines
to turn to stone again,
and marries an anti-
quarian named Tom-
kins. Her after - fate
isn’t recorded ; but pre-
sumably she becomes a
serious-minded taxpayer,
and brings up a large
family.
# # #
Mrs. Brough as
Niobe, and her husband
as Niobe’s bailee, Peter
Dunn, have the humour
of the show pretty much
to themselves. They area
wonderful pair, the statu-
esque Greek lady in a
costume of some un-
fathomable period 8.0.,
and the hot and flurried
product of the nineteenth
century; and between
them they prod uce a series
of sensations. Nobody
on the Australian stage
eyer did look quite so,
distracted and perspiring
as Mr. Brough in critical moments, and in
the present case he rises to frenzy most
of the time. The altogether too beautiful Cecil
Ward has a good part as the modern youth who
tries to explain the theory of pants to the Greek,
and fills it with the best results. The haggard
maiden relative, compounded of vinegar and front
teeth, whom Niobe wants to have most particu-
larly blasted by the gods, is held up to artistic
execration by Miss Romer ; Mr. Boucicault has a
small part as the antiquarian who finally takes the
image to his manly chest; and the rest of the
copipauy do nothing calling for special admoni-
tion or reproach. A comparatively new lady is
memorable for the fact that her evening dress is
cut lower in front than any other dress on record ;
but, despite this drawback, or perhaps partly
because of it, “Niobe ” is an assured success of the
largest kind. Mr. W. H. Wallace is manager pro
tern, and his bright smile occupies the front door
until Mr. Mayne’s return.
** * *
Mr. Howe, of Sydney Royal, has dropped
into authorship, and his good-natured partner, Mr.
Baker, has been induced to rise in a weak moment
and assist in putting it on the stage. The name of
the thing is “Light Ahead,” and the person who
assisted Mr. Howe in its composition is alleged to
be one H. Q. Hanon. Possibly this is an assumed
name, and is intended to express the writer’s
desire to be Hanonymous. There are four acts in
“Light Ahead,’’ and it is one of those strictly
conventional pieces in which everybody shakes
hands violently on the smallest provocation. The
principal character is a marine captain with a most
unusual tendency to embrace bis boatswain on deck
at all times and seasons, This isn’t charapteristic
of the ordinary captain. He is beguiled into a boat
by a wicked doctor and a lawyer, and banged on
the head with an oar, also he is shot and thrown
overboard, but the boatswain rescues him, dead
but not wounded, though with marks of violence
upon him. Then, in some unexplained fashion,
the doctor, instead of being gaoled, is allowed to
hustle the captain into a lunatic asylum; but he
escapes, and though his whereabouts is known
and orders are issued for his re-capture, he isn’t
caught, owing to the warlike attitude of the funny
man. Later on an heiress adopts the deranged
victim, and keeps him in the doctor’s house,
and that iron miscreant seemingly doesn’t dare to
throw him out. Last of all, the doctor is arrested
by all the rest of the company for burgling his own
premisesand breaking inti his own safe. The Co.,
in fact, has taken possession of the premises by
forcible breakage and entry, and the real owner,
instead of having them all run in, makes his mid-
night irruption humbly and apologetically by his
own window, and is grabbed by his own minion in
buttons.
* * *
Sundry people who deserve a much better
fate are wasted on this lamentable show. Mrs.
Molyueux is always excellent in a nicely-dressed
part, and it is only when she is called upon to
play a cheap calico heroine with broken boots that
she seems to lose heart. In the present case she
has a nicely-dressed part, Mr. Flirnmer, hitherto
known chiefly as a good young man bearing up
against misfortunes, gets a ebanee as the
principal evening-dress murderer, and ean give
points in many respects even to that celebrated and
experienced assassin, Mr. Jewett. Also Messrs.
Lesttin and Baker have much to recommend them,
but the latter gentleman is hereby urged to abjure
the pot hat and frock-coat style of architecture as
being unsuited to his variety of beauty. The
author rages around as the hero, and strikes
many graceful attitudes with his legs, and
various other people are a burden upon the soul
of a rather thin audience. The scenery, by Mr.
MR. WILLIAM LARMOUR.
(Coogee Palace Aquarium),
Vaughan, suggests that that gentleman will yet
rise to greatness, if he hasn’t got there already.
** * »
Smythe (says a Melb. paper) “ goes to
England to capture Melba.” The Bulletin begs
to enquire why the Most Travelled doesn’t seek
to capture John Burns for an Australian lecturing-
season. Burns, who has no money and has, up to
date, been too earnest to accept from the people
anything more than bare necessaries, could make
himself honestly independent for life by a turn
round the Australias, besides rebuilding his health
and giving the Australian Labour-party a mighty
impetus. And what a reception he would get
from men, women, and children ! The Bulletin
commends the idea to Smythe—and to Burns.
** * *
“ Miss Esmeralda ” will continue to fare-
well at Sydney Lyceum for an indefinite period.
Up to date business is remarkably good, especially
considering the busted state of the city, and here
and there certain suspicions of originality are
visible about Lonnen and one or two other
characters. The two big minstrel shows at the
Tivoli and the Opera House still pursue the fleet-
ing dollar with vigour and overtake that elusive
coin quite frequently, and several smaller shows of
the same order overtake coins of humbler value.
Also various deserving entertainments in aid of
the Queensland flood sufferers are on foot and
new ones are constantly arising.
* # *■ *
“ Hoodman Blind,” at Sydney Majesty’s,
seems to have girded up its loins for a long
struggle against the ravages of time. Also, the
fact that the new leading lady, Miss Lilia Wilde,
is a lasting success is becoming every day more
deeply embedded in the public consciousness.
The Bulletin’s enthusiasm over her is based
chiefly on the fact that, when she is cursed and
ejected by her husband, with an insane glare in
his eyes and blood on his shirt-front, she contrives
to look just as a lady would look in real life if she
found herself thrown on to the cold, unfeeling
door-mat at an instant’s notice. The present
writer has three times seen a lady removed by the
hair, at short notice —once on suspicion of infi-
delity, once for selling her husband’s clothes for
drink, and the other time owing to religious dif-
ferences, and each time she looked very like Miss
Wilde and very unlike most other actresses. She
was too much astonished for hysterics, and too
short of breath to bellow in the customary stage
fashion. Miss Wilde could not be more realistic
even had she been thrown out by ah inhospitable
step-father in the days of her childhood, and so
learned her part from personal experience*
“Pepita,” the least familiar of the Firm’s
stock operas, was revived at Melbourne Princess’
on Saturday night, when a moderately crowded
audience showed symptoms of fatigue towards the
finish. “ Pepita ” is a melodious narrative con-
taining no hero, no love interest, and no strikingly
original character save a black bull, which doesn’t
appear on the scene until death has closed its eye-
lids, and the dress-circle is preparing to go. A
pair of wives, two husbands and a couple of arch-
conspirators carry the plot along to the closing
scene, and the stuffed bull provides a happy
climax. The wives, as may be remembered, are
in the habit of leaving home from Saturday to
Monday under the protection of a large
military person, whilst the husbands stop behind
and wonder what is happening during the inter-
val. Ultimately it transpires that one of the
wives is making arrangements to claim the local
crown, but a second military person, who seems to
hold the kingdom in trust for a rival next-of-kin*
comes along with an army of six soldiers, or more,
and places the wrong wife under arrest, conse-
quently the direct succession remains in an unset-
tled s! ab ■ until the husband of the right wife casts
away Ins ice-cream can, and settles the matter by
slaughtering the stuffed bull aforesaid, whereupon
the populace break out into a chorus and all is
well. Regarded as a lesson in monarchial govern-
ment, “ Pepita ’* is somewhat wild and inconclu-
sive, but it commands respect on account of a
diplomatic duet between the two soldier plotters
in the second act. This duet has lost a
good deal of its early charm, inasnutch as
Bombardos (Ryley) and Pataques (Lauri) are not
as sharply contrasted as they used to be, but it
still stands good for a treble encore. Otherwise,
neither Ryley nor Lauri is a huge success,
Laumane, too, is by no means as funny as he
seems to suppose, and even the trustworthy
Howard Vernon betrays his trust a good deal on
this occasion. Violet Varley and Flora Graupner
(the latter of whom seems to have lost some of her
old voice) are small, physically and artistically, as
the untrammelled spouses, and the background
characters make very little difference either way.
With the exception of some aggressive water-
lilies, which resemble dumplings at anchor on a
silent stream, “Pepita” is an elegant spectacle,
however. On Saturday next they produce “ The
Gondoliers ” for the eighth time, or thereabouts.

The fugitive opera season at the Royal
closed with a sickly benefit on Saturday, and the
young company are now understood to be picking
up polish and searching after dramatic fire in the
provinces. On April Ist, the enterprising Darrell
will hoist his flag at this establishment. Sound
actors are plentiful and cheap in Melbourne just
now, so George has a really promising chance to
reopen the old house in a style worthy of its
traditions,
** * *
Fillis’ Circus will reluctantly quit its pre-
sent site after Monday (March 6th). Till then the
Nubian lions are warranted to show their teeth at
each performance; also a remorseless fiend is
dropping millions of circus handbills around the
suburbs, and the Melbourne landscape is dotted
oyer with bright green intimations that Fillis’
scale of charges has been reduced.
** * *
Frank Clark, who recently took a farewell
benefit, has decided to “play-up” the proceeds at
the Opera House instead of departing for
America. As a rule, the man who means to go
away intends to change his mind, so this intima-
tion carries very little shock with it. For the
nonce the Opera House will be known as the
Alhambra Palace of Varieties, veteran John
Wallace is engaged to stage-manage sundry
novelties in ballets, and prices of admission range
from the humble sixpence upwards.
** * *
The trustees of Melb. Exhibition have
decided not to permit any more baby-shows in the
building. The sages who have the “letting-out ”
of the white elephant seem to soent some im-
proper connection between baby-shows and ladies’
tugs-of-war. Presently they will draw the line at
everything, save wood-chopping tournaments and
hallelujah hug-mugging.

Max O.’Rell lias captivated the Maori'
landers, except in Dunedin, where his lecture
on “Sandy” raised Sodom and Gomorrah, and
produced such results that Max fled for his
life. At Wellington such houses had not been
seen for years. The famous frog-eater was
gradually making bis way north, and the tour was
to close at Auckland on 24th, but it has been
so, successful that the Most Travelled Smythe will
probably engage Max for some other country
where there will be no counter-attractions in the
shape of want-of-confidence motions, or trials of
bank directors, or quasi-charity entertainments in
which everybody gets something except the
beneficial re.

Sass and Emery’s comedy company has
been doing very good work in Adelaide, but their
reward has been only the consciousness of virtue.
“Caste” had a run of three nights, and on 22nd
Feb. “The Late Lamented” was staged. It is.
supposed that the title of. the piece refers to the
erstwhile patrons of the theatre.
** * *
Mark Twain’s summing-up of that hoary
fiction, the North-Amerioan Indian, is brought
back to memory by the miserable performances of
somebody’s aboriginal troupe from Queensland.
In the course of their show at Melbourne Ex-
hibition the “Wild Australians” managed to
cover themselves with contumely directly they
took their clothes off. The chief warrior of the
crowd proved incapable of shooting an arrow from,
a bow into a gigantic target at 15 yards, and even
raised a question as to whether he could hit a hay-
stack. The spear-flinging of the whole party was
a ghastly mockery of all pre-conceived notions
concerning native skill with native weapons.
Furthermore, one of the humbugs chosen to
prove hia wild dexterity in firing a pointed stick
along an asphalted walk, fired so crooked that
his stick landed in the foot of a bystander who
tottered off to the hospital cursing the incapacity
of “a fast-dying race.” ' y

The accompanying portrait represents Mr. William I ir-
mour, lessee and manager of Coogee Palace Aquarium
Sydney. Mr. Larmour was born at Eden, Oarrickter.. U s W
land, on March 18,1847. In his early days he took aleadim'
part in various literary and debating societies and w"t
intended for the ministry of the Irish I’resbyttri
Church. Mr. Larmour was a diligent student until In
years of age, when his father’s health was so indifferent
that he decided to relieve him from any further exuerisr-
on his behalf. Lord Annesley, requiring a head master
for one of his schools, selected Mr. Lannonr from amoiwst
many applicants for the position. After four years how-
ever, they had various differences over Mr. Gladstone’s
Irish Land Bill and the Act for the Disestablishment of
the Irish Church, Mr. Larmour being a warm advocate of
the measures, and as a consequence he resigned his position
and was immediately appointed head master of the school
in the district in which he was born. Among his punils were
the families of various leading Irishmen. Dur'ng the eb bt
years which he spent near Belfast he was, until leaving the
scholastic profession, editor of the Irish Educational
Journal, and a constant contributor to several daily
papers, as well as the Ulster representative of the Central
News Agency. When the British Association visited
Belfast Mr. Larmour was selected by the Northern Whig
the leading local paper, to summarise the papers read’
and the debates ensuing thereon, in the mathematical and
physical section. This was a recognition of his ability
as a mathematician, and lie was honoured by a special
vote of thanks from the committee of the association
for the accuracy of his reports. Mr. Larmour was also ai
this time in charge of the Income-tax office for the
county, and the town of Oarrickfergus and neighbouring
districts. In 1876 the then managing director of th?
Belfast Ropework Co. induced Mr. Larmour to become a
member of the company, and so leave scholastic for
commercial life. After spending a short time
in the works he took to the road, and for eleven
yqqrs was a prominent factor in making his concern one
of the largest and most prosperous in the world.
In the interests of his company he visited almost, even-
country in each of the five continents. His last business
journey covered a distance of over 100,000 miles. He
still retains his connection with the Belfast Ropework
Company. The cause of his remaining in Australia for
the last six years was that, being induced to enter into a
number of speculations in which he had over £12,000 at
stake, he preferred to personally watch his interests
Most of the money has now vanished like that of manv
other confiding investors, but he is still watching the
remainder. In September, 1890, he leased the Cooo-ee
Palace Aquarium, and though then a novice "in
catering for the public amusement, his business ability
and tact have made a place pay where the professional
showman hitherto failed to make both ends meet. Mr.
Larmour is well-known to the commercial community,
and his name and face are very familiar to the many
thousands who \ isit his charming resort by the sea. Mr.
Larmour gained great popularity a few weeks ago by his
unpremeditated inroad into the den of lions at Fillis’
circus. He is now promoting a brass band contest to
take place at his Aquarium on Good Friday and Saturday
for prizes amounting to £B5.
At Poverty Point.
Young Leo Byrne, of Queensland (late “ Ned’s Chum ”),
will have a part in “ Becket,” to be produced by Irving at
London Lyceum as a dress-rehearsal for America.
Christie Murray has been regularly paying young Leo
Byrne (son of “Bobby” Byrne, of Brisbane Punch) a
retaining-fee of £2 weekly when not acting, which has
been the case for some time, “Ned’s Chum” having been
a frost. Christie will, therefore, be all the richer now
that Irving has taken the clever boy in hand.
The proprietors of the last Melbourne baby-show lost
£5OO over the speculation.
Baby-shows are getting stale. How would an exhibition
of aged females go for a novelty ?
A good, upstanding lie When Paderewski, the pianist,
was performing at a concert lately, a lady, carried away
by enthusiasm, rushed upon the platform, and prostrated
herself at his feet. The eminent musician gazed at her
in an abstracted way for a moment, and then absent-
mindedly wiping his boots on her back hair, went on
playing.

have seen the light at Genoa last month, was withdrawn,
owing to the wretchedness of the artists provided by the
impressario. Cowen is going to try Milan.

Charles Warner, replying to a critic who asserts the
frequency of unhappy marriages in the profession,
instances the Kendals, the Comptons, the four Terrys,
the Macklins, and Thornes, as proof to the contrary.
Overwhelming ! Fancy a pleader digging out half-a-
dozen names to prove such a case in any other profession.
Warner takes the exceptions for the rule, that’s all.
Now that Jeremiah Brown has finally collapsed, how
does the Cyclorama Co. stand in case of an action for the
recovery of the public gate-money ? People paid to see a
Waterloo veteran aged 101, and now ic appears that they
only saw an old frost, of 82 who had never been near
Waterloo in his life. Is I his false pretences or what ?

Harry Paulton is building another farcical comedy in
collaboration with his son. “ Niobe ” was the work of
the old man and his offspring, but nobody knows for cer-
tain which of the pair cribbed the idea of (lie plot, and
whicli of them wrote the plain dialogue.
“ Niobe ” is the latest of those statue plays which ever
fascinate. Shukespeare’s “ Hermione ”is the first of the
Thespian living statues, and then Shirley’s, from which
Shiei took “ Evadne.” “The Marble Heart ” and “ Pyg*
nmlion and Galatea” recur to mind.

The assumption of Premier G. Republican
Dibbs that the name of the new Governor of
N.S. Wales would be submitted to him in
advance for approval has proved erroneous.
The haughty stand taken by the Colonial
Office in this matter, even after the sharp
lesson given it by MTlwraith in the Blake
affair, is a logical consequence of the ex-
Republican’s in accepting a
title while intoxicated with the glamour of
the Court.

N.B. —Every precaution has been taken to
secure absolute secrecy and fairness, and to other-

of the reputation of the paper.
Many replies in Competition No. 1 came to
hand too late chiefly owing to mail-delays
through Queensland floods. All these have been
returned. To fully permit of answers being re
