SOHO SQUARE, LONDON.
The Wild-Cat Column.
The Land Co. of Australia, about the largest
oncern of its kind in this continent, has resolved to
Houidate. The company’s properties are set down at
£636 000, and the mortgages on them amount to
fi £426 000 only.” Alas! the kind of property which is
. lued'at £636,000, generally realises £200,000 almost
jhese times, and, judging by past experience, the Co.
l % strike an unusual patch "of luck if its land yields
enough to pay mortgages. There are also “ other
liabilities, £145,000;” the miscellaneous assets are mostly
mortgaged ; and some uncertainty seems to prevail as to
whether the uncalled capital is not also up the spout.
But the utter rottenness of the Land Co. of Australia is
demonstrated, most of all, by the reason assigned for the
“ ,lapse. This institution was one of those which staved
nff their creditors for three years under the Companies
Arrangement Act. It has now had a year’s grace in
whiobto get its assets together, and it shuts up because
it can’t pay even the first instalment now coming due.
Up to date, so far as The Bulletin is aware, all the com-
panies which gained time under the same Act have gone
smash thesame way. The Act has enabled quite a lot of them
to fence with their creditors, and to run riot among the
deposits a while longer, but it hasn’t kept one concern
afloat, because they were almost all so rotten that even
three centuries grace, let alone three years, could hardly
have raised them to solvency.
* * *
The gifted Theodore Fink, lawyer, poet and
land-boomer, has entertained the Melb. Bankers’ Institute
with a sound moral lecture concerning the evil effects of
Foreign Loans upon a Young Democracy. One extract
from Theo. will suffice to show the extent to which he has
repented of his past life : “ Foreign loans are, with very
few exceptions, an unmitigated curse. Usually they
arrive in Victoria in the very class of commodities that
incite to waste and extravagance. As tending to involve
a small population in a young country in a degrading
form of interest, servitude, and practically involving,
as they do, the gradual transference of the
real ownership of the soil to residents in other countries,
the resulting benefits are wholly inadequate to counter-
balance these evils.” There were tears in Theodore’s
voice as he touched upon the curse of pawning city and
suburban allotments to residents in other countries. One
almost expected him to kneel and pray right there before
the Bankers! Institute—“ O, Lord! ,put back Thy
universe and give me yesterday.” It was a most affecting
address throughout, eloquent, earnest, breathing a gentle
financial piety. # #
Great anxiety is expressed in Brisbane as to the
funds of the Government Savings Bank, the greater part
of which have been divided among three banks all of
whose shares are selling considerably below par.

land National Bank, £4 13s. ; Royal, £3 18s. ; Bank of
N O £2 Is. And who is buying?

# * *
The Silverton Tramway (N.S.W.) Co., with a
capital of £200,000, made last year £82,155 profit, of which
£59,341 was paid in dividends. And all this, though the
strike cost them £40,000 !
* # #
One of Melbourne’s prosperous suburban drapers
recently did what was considered a smart thing by put-
ting up his shutters for a day .or two, in order to bill
them with the announcement that he had “gone bung ! ”
This was merely a device to fetch the public to his little
sale, where they fought for “ alarming sacrifices” under
the delusion that a ruthless bank was realising the wily
draper’s stock at any price. Melbourne commerce sees
nothing discreditable in a solvent’ trader shamming bank-
ruptcy, and it is now quite usual for a pushing firm to
advertise a company sale every month.
# • *
The Mt. Morgan G.M. Co. gave £lOOO towards
the flood-relief fund. This represents just about the nett
profit on one day’s work.

Gov. Jersey has insured the life of his eldest
son for £lO,OOO, and the lives of his two daughters for
£2OOO apiece, in the Australian Mutual Provident.

brewery.
# # *
The busted-bank victim learns nothing from the
lessons of the past. The late manager of the Federal—
we have nothing to say against him personally—is
winding it up in quite the early Daviesian style. It is
about time that a man who files his schedule should
appoint himself his own trustee and fix his own dividend.
# # *
The Victorian Crown Law Department has
changed its charge against the Mercantile Bank directors
from that of “issuing” a false balance-sheet to one of
“conspiring together to issue a false balance-sheet.”
This apparently trifling alteration in the charge is worth
noticing in connection with the alleged gist of Matthew
Davies’ defence. Davies, it is understood, will advance
the excuse that he, as Speaker of the House, was too
deeply immersed in Parliamentary duties to be capable of
giving his mind to the business of the Mercantile Bank,
and was not, therefore, in a position to test the correct-
ness of its balance-sheets. This interesting case will in no
way touch upon the system of one Davies institution pay-
ing in a thumping big cheque to another Davies institu-
tion just previous to the close of the half-year. It will,
or should, be the simplest of cases. The balance-sheet
was incompatible with the bank’s failure a fortnight after
issue. Therefore, the sheet was falsa, unless the failure
cropped up by accident in the meantime. Either the
directors were concerned in the issue of this balance-
sheet, or unconcerned, and if unconcerned, why so ?
Somebody did it, and if somebody be boiled down to
Manager Millidge (the auditors counting for no more
than the uninformed directors) the case collapses, inas-
much as no one man can form a conspiracy. Yet the pre-
liminary hearing is “ expected to last a fortnight.”
* * #
The O’Loghlen “indignantly repudiates” the
suggestion that the present Victorian Government
will do all in its power to shield busted bank-directors. It
js a fact that the new Crown has engaged Simpson, the
blunt and keen legal accountant, to assist in preparing
evidence for the Mercantile Bank prosecution. The
Brown of Shiels’ time would have nothing to do with
Simpson, and declined to allow his small claim for pro-
fessional services in working up the prima-fncie case.
* * #
N.S.W. Gold Extraction Co. is liquidating—
another victim of the yarn about gold being left in the
tailings. It was formed to treat 30,000 tons (from stone
which yielded many thousand ozs. gold) at Parkes, the
accumulation of the old local quartz crushing co.
Samples brought to Sydney were treated with dazzling
results, and now the whole concern—Huntingdon mill,
tailings, engine, boiler and all are on sale by tender.
Another warning against the tailings hummer.
# # #
The Metals Extraction Go., with their ammonia
process, started out to work a big miracle with Junction
North silver ore. While the works were building every-
thing glittered. The cleaning-up showed 1600 oz. from 70
tons (an unofficial report says more) ore, which assayed
40oz. Shares at once fell 50 per cent. And it is this pro-
cess which is going to redeem White Rock 1
* * *
A doleful half-yearly report from the Last Call
mine. Continuous prospecting work, nothing payable
(you might as well prospect in Collins-street or Sydney
Domain), and finances will not allow work being carried
on more than a few weeks, but whatever course is ex-
pedient in the interests, &c., will be adopted. “ All the
machinery is in good order. We smile ! Again—why not
put the little money left into Adams’ sweep ? It was bad
enough to pay £lO,OOO for half the so-called mine, but
what excuse can be given for having absolutely thrown
away another £lO,OOO in “ development? ” Of course the
money was put up for this purpose, but surely expendi-
ture should have ceased the moment the swindle was
proved.
* * #
The Peak Hill paper still harps on the gold-in-
tailings theory, and quotes Dr. Curran re the similarity of
the ores of Mount Morgan, Pambula, and Peak Hill. If
the gold is in the tailings it would be easy to prove the
fact. Many a big body worth half-an-ounoe only has been
misjudged to contain an oz. The writer has waited half
a life-time to see a large body, estimated at an oz., go 2oz.
Rumour says that an official report casting a complete
damper on the Peak Hill discovery has yet to see the light.
* * *
The keen and active canvassing for two seats on
the Golden Gully (N.S.W.) board demonstrates the
anxiety in these bad times to earn even directors’ fees.
Personation was, it is alleged, practised to some extent.

son. If Lansell long continues to receive the lion’s share
of the Bendigo dividends he will again become a
millionaire.

A par. in the “Wild-Cat ” column, of Feb. 25,
began thus: —“ The last of the Victoria Coffee Palace Co.
was brightened by,” &c., &c. The printer carefully
omitted the word “meeting,” which makes a difference.
The Coffee Palace hasn’t come to its latter end.
# * *
Some wonderful alluvial gold has been found
near Gundagai; much of the ground taken up and floated
two years back on the strength of Lindley’s lead has been
re-pegged. Bindley made some thousands out of his
claim and then died". His widow has leased the ground
to some Sydney men, who have put up a plant for
hydraulic sluicing.
# * •
The chairman of the Broken Hill South mine
declares that the average weekly earnings of the miners
are £4 11s. 7d. each.
* • •
Mining Standard : “ It is a crying shame that
some energetic persons should not take a hand in the
erection of a cyanide plant suitable for the treatment of
Pambula ore.” Now, although the Australian Gold Re-
covery Co., which holds theeyanide rights, has big capital
and has already spent much in N.S.W. mines,
including Pambula, it has never erected a plant itself.
# * »
What appears a recurrence of the rich patches of
gold-bearing rock found in the Faulkner and Pambula
mines is now being worked in the Mount Lewison claim,
owned by a Sydney syndicate. The last report says that
they are bagging 25-oz. ore, and, like the rest of them,
putting aside second and third-class rock. Rich gold-
finds are seldom permanent, and the bitter experience of
the Pambula must make one question uppermost—How
long will it last?
* • #
Broken Hill South looks up : bank overdraft,
£17,000, reduced to £4250, and total liabilities from
£40,000 to £10,000; cost of smelting also lowered a few
shillings. Dividends hoped for; at any rate, no call
looms.
« # *
One of the biggest permanent dividend-paying
mines of Bendigo is the New Red White and Blue, whose
last cake was 1071 ozs. from 1015 tons.
» * *
The finding of large, sharp-cornered fragments
of lode formation assaying as high as 60 per cent, metallic
tin in one of the faces of Mt. Bischoff (Tas.) indicate that
the source of the greatest alluvial tin-deposit found in
Australia may yet be discovered in the mine itself. It
has alwavs been an enigma where the deposit came from,
inasmuch as it lies above and not below the surrounding
country.
# • •
As the writer has long anticipated, the London
shareholders of the True Blue mine, Croydon, are
grievously dissatisfied with the manner in which the
management of the mine has been conducted. They
accuse the directorate of misleading conduct.
* # *
The Tasmanian G. M. Co., for many years the
biggest dividend mine of the island, and out of which
some big fortunes have been made, has now an overdraft
of nearly £50,000.
• # *
The much-vaunted Lockwood-Chapple(“hypher-
phoric”) process has been applied to the Sir Walter Scott
ore, which, we were at first told, was worth 3 to 4ozs.
per' ton; now, it is less than 2ozs., “ and the better stone is
under water.” Never a word about the inferior quality of
the gold! The “ hypherpheric ” is remarkably slow;
that which is slow, requiring power and fuel, cannot be
economical. However, the writer suspends further
judgment concerning it pending a satisfactory critical
examination by some practical mining man.
# # *
If shareholders placed any reliance on mining
managers’ reports there would be a boom in the Argen-
tine shares. The last speaks of the striking of several
silver lodes, the richest assaying 200 ozs. and 60 per cent.
lead, and the discovery of an enormous body of auriferous
quartz, yielding 12s to 245. worth of gold per ton. This is
the assay value according to the letter received. The
report adds: “At present it is impossible to profitably
dispatch to Vic. or S. A. any Argentine ores yielding less
than 150 ozs. to the ton, whereas local treatment should
secure a profit on 30 oz. ore.” Why expect the public to
believe such a yarn ? The difference between 30 and 150
ozs. represents nearly £2O a ton, to say nothing of the
cost of treatment. If melted locally, coke must be sent to
the mine, the bullion brought back, and, if lead will not
pay to carry, then the metal must be refined on the mine.
The ghastly truth is seldom told. If the Argentine mines
were near the seaboard they would be valuable, but like
many other far inland silver mines, carriage almost ki Is
them. If they could be removed to Newcastle all would
be right; but a body of quartz, however big, which assays
only 3 dwts is of little use.
» # *
Sydney D.T. : “It is believed that the problem
of treating the low-grade ores at Broken Hill has now
been solved.” Presumably it means the sulphide
problem. Who believes it? The D.T., or the man with
the particular process referred to? The solution of the
sulphide problem has been so often announced during the
last five years that nobody believes it, nor will any
sensible man take it for granted until he sees it with his
own eyes.
* # *
Smelting at Mount Oostigan has re-commenced.
The proprietor is about to build two reverberatory
furnaces to keep things going right ahead.
» # *
A director visiting the Rockdale mine, N.S.W.,
is pleased with everything. The reef averages five feet,
and is broken for ss. a ton, which will be reduced to 3s.
as soon as the tunnel intersects it. He adds that the new
ideas of the manager for saving very fine gold have been
a great success. These are the use of tepid water to keep
the silver lively—a very good, but very old, idea ; two
sprinklers over the plates, also a useful, but by no means
recent notion, and the use of an incurved splash copper
plate immediately outside the boxes, a thing most mining
men have seen.
* # *
So it is publicly admitted, at last, that the Mt.
Wells tin-field, Victoria, is a failure. It had its boom
three years ago when anything would boom with a little
lying and misrepresentation. On these things Wells
lived, moved and had its boom. Of course the machinery,
on which no expense was spared, was condemned before
the mines. They are always all right until the death.

ing rooms at a big hotel at eight guineas a week.

i-i the suspended Anglo-Australran I. F. and L. Co. Presi-

A deposit of brown coal has been struck at
Williamstown, Melb. As the cost of freight will be very
light the Co. which has leased the land ought to make a
good thing out of it. A bit of a rush set in, but the Co.
had all the available ground pegged out beforehand.

—appearing in the balance-sheet.
# * •
A West Australian digger, when shifting quar-
ters, owing to the local scarcity of water, the other day,
found £l2OO worth of gold in the centre-peg hole of his
tent.
* * *
Danver Powers, F.G.S., &c., &c., discourses
learnedly on the stamper-battery, and argues plausibly in
favour of roller-mills. The world judges crushing-
machinery by results. If roller-mills are superior, why,
when preparations are made for extensive crushing, is the
stamper-mill always adopted—as on Broken Hill, and, in
fact, all over the world ? Why is Lansell, a man without
prejudices, spending £15,000 on a hundred-head stamper-
mill on Bendigo? The stampers’ defects—waste power
and indiscriminate action—are well known, but other
more serious defects are discovered when other mills are
tried.
When the flood-waters rushed down the Gympie
mines, a number of them blew up with deafening reports,
as if the town was being bombarded.
# # #
The Pennyweight Hill Sluicing Party, Vic.,
have completed their race, four and a half miles, partly
through hard rock, and have cleaned up 9Jozs. from 1000
loads. There are many obstacles: the fall is small, water
scarce, pressure low, and price of water high (£-2 per
million gals.—four million gals, per week are required,
and they have been using two). This once obtained, also
6in. pipes to give proper pressure, and the party have a
good chance ahead. They have undertaken a big job,
but they have unlimited dirt of the expected value.
# # *
One of the biggest gold-mines in the world is
Homestake of the Black Hills of U.S. It has a low-grade
free milling ore, and by the employment of a great army
of men and the most powerful machinery it has an output
of nearly £1,000,000 of gold yearly.
# # * *
Generally the newspapers of Australia have lost
a gigantic opportunity. The history of the world’s biggest
journals shows that their fame was never made by grada-
tions. In all long-established communities, newspapers
are never born great nor achieve greatness by reason of
long life ; they become great by reason of seizing a big
opportunity. The exclusive publication of the declaration
of American Independence made one ; the Bulgarian
atrocities a second; incomparablenesi in war corres-
pondence another. Like opportunities occur in Australia,
but they are generally missed, and the biggest has been the
lettingoutof the “innards' ’on the land-and-bogus-bank and
miningboom. Where, from one end of Australia to another,
was there a daily metropolitan newspaper that made it its
business to expose the fraud practised by the alleged men
of money and power in the name of boom ? Not one pre-
dicted the crash until it had arrived. The papers flourished
on advts. and tempered the wind to the shorn lamb.
Directly or indirectly they were all more or less in it. In
Australian history it will be written down as the brief age
of palpable fraud in which dense newspaper blindness was
caused by unwillingness to see.
The legislative Thugs of N.S.W. Council
have deleted from the Elections Bill the clause
compelling employers to let their hands go to the
polls in working hours when necessary.
Barton’s reason for, not prosecuting the
locked-out Sydney bootmakers for conspiracy
could, perhaps, have been boiled down to much less
than half-a-column. Thus : Once bitten, twice
shy.
Dibbs is making no threats, but he
intends, it is understood, to re-submit to the
N.S.W. Legislative Council the “ vote-for-thrift ”
clause inserted in the Electoral Bill by that body.
If it still asserts the principle, he will call a
conference of both Houses when the bill returns to
the Assembly. That failing, hi; will prorogue
Parliament for 24 hours, flood the Council, and
send the bill back to it, as it originally left the
Assembly.
In re the National Ass assertion that the
N.S.W. Labour members are mere self-seekers,
“living on the game,” let it be remembered that
the party long ago carried a resolution to accept
no offices of emolument during the existence of
the present Parliament. What is not so generally
known is that, when the ballot for the Works
Committee was coming on, the Labour members
were asked to nominate two men to run for the
places with the liberal Protectionist selections.
The request refused, two men were privately ap-
proached for the same purpose, and they also
refused. How many men in the House, outside
the Labour-party, would have thus calmly given
up about =£7oo or =£Boo yearly ?

Any purchaser of The Bulletin who can
correctly supply the word missing from the para-
graph on the subjoined coupon—which coupon is
to be cut out and forwarded to The Bulletin,
accompanied by one shilling, will be paid the total
amount received in response to this advertisement.
Should there be more than one successful com-
petitor, the total amount received will be divided
amongst such successful competitors pro rata.
Coupons will be received at The Bulletin
Office within five weeks from Saturday, 25th Feb.,
1893. Should any coupon other than this be used,
the sender will be disqualified.
The missing word is placed in a sealed envelope
in the custody of the manager of the Bank of
New Zealand, Sydney, and the contents are
known only to the manager of this paper.
When the prizes are awarded, the winning
word and the names and addresses of the success-
ful competitors will be published in The Bulle-
tin.
Should it happen that no one succeeds in naming
the word, the whole of the money will be added
to that of the succeeding competition, particulars
of which will duly appear.
The sum of one shilling may be forwarded by
Post-office order or stamps of any province; when
the latter are sent, an additional penny to be
added for exchange.

Contractor Amos owns a hill composed
entirely of the splendid Bowral stone, of which
that unique and imposing building, the New
Equitable Life offices in Oeorge-street, Sydney, is
being constructed. He bought it some years ago
for .£3500, and has since refused for it, it is said,
twelve times that amount. The Equitable build-
ing should make a boom in Bowral stone.

Dr. George Bennett, F.L.S., who was first
secretary to the Australian Museum, Sydney,
entered upon his 90th year, Feb. 1.
Governor Duff’(N.S.W.) is a great grand-
son of the great-great grandfather of the son-in-
law of the Prince of Wales. Think hard !
Robert Louis Stevenson, now on his way
to Sydney, advises Britain to annex Samoa.
Robert owns a little property there, it is said.
A present supposedly pauper inmate of the
Liverpool (N.S.W.) Poor Asylum, some few years
ago, purchased an estate in Ireland for £11,150.
The fallen Matthew Davies lives in a little
one-storey stucco villa at Caulfield, strangely like
the one at St. Helena, pointed out as Napoleon’s.
W. A. Horn, Gov. Kintore’s friend and
financial adviser, is worth, at least, £500,000 —the
Horn of plenty. He has finally abandoned
politics.
Thomas Elder, the millionaire enthusiast
in respect to Australian exploration, is suffering
from senile decay, and it is difficult to catch him
in a lucid interval.
Squatter Glass (W.A.), who shot his two
grown-up daughters and then suicided, was a son
of the historical Victorian squatter, Hugh Glass —
“Broken ” Glass—of Flemington.
Mrs. Terry, a native of N.S.W., who
lately died at Oampbelltown, left 180 living
descendants behind her. Her sister, aged 93, still
living at Albury (N.S.W.) has 198 living descen-
dants.
The octogenarian Captain Battye, who
retires from the superintendency of Wagga police
upon the full pay of £5OO a year, and an Imperial
Army pension of £lOO, began life as a page to
Queen Adelaide.
Ex - branch - manager Bennett,
who stole £lO,OOO or so from the Lon-
don Chartered Bank, at Sydney, got
ten years, which would unquestionably
be far too much to give to any man for
a simple act of stealing, even if it
amounted to a million. But Judge
Fitzhardinge obviously didn’t inflict so
tremendous a punishment on the mere
thief, but upon the cold-blooded
Sunday-school hypocrite who lay perdu
and allowed his innocent subordinate,
Wilgoss, to be sent to rot in prison.
The statement that the brilliant
“ Mudgee ” Taylor, ex-M.P. (N.S.W.),
is qualifying to become a missionary to
the South African niggers is a canard.
Mudgee is just now managing-director
of the Echo Farm Home for Inebriates.
He has been thoroughly converted,
and preserves the most vigorous
Christian discipline in his domains.
For instance, no swearing is allowed.
If a man says “Damn !” Mudgee in-
stantly gives him two Beecham’s pills
—worth a guinea a box.
The Earl of Portarlington, who
died recently in England, was cele-
brated chiefly as having been —in his
younger days and before he became a
peer—the prize drunkard of the British
House of Commons. He represented
a pocket borough with 14 electors, and
is reputed never to have been seen
altogether sober ; but nobody took any
official notice of bis chronic muddlerr.ent
until one day when he shook hands
cordially with the Queen, and told her
that her face seemed familiar, doncher-
know, but he had quite forgotten her
name.
Lindsay Cooper, night-overseer of S.A.
Register, has been on the paper since 1836, when
he arrived as an apprentice with the founder,
Robert Thomas, and assisted to bring out the first
number in a tent at Holdfast Bay.
John Sweet, who died at Newcastle
(N.S.W.), on February 20, aged 75, came to
Sydney in ’47, and was one of the captors of bush-
ranger Wilson, on Warland’s Range, near Mur-
rurundi. He was an old Maitlander.
Dear Bulletin, —Re explorers. It is not
generally known, perhaps, that Wright, third in
command of the Burke and Wills expedition, the
man who “delayed,” is a well-known figure in
Sydney, and said to be a wealthy land-owner.
—J.F.

he’s the First Lord of the Treasury. Orchestra —

A member of the N.S.W. Legislative
Council publicly ate, at one meal recently, two
plates of soup, two plates of meat and vegetables,
one help of pudding, and two whole loa'es of
bread, with butter. He ought to have a vote for
thrift; the bread and butter were not charged for.

Says the Australian Star: “The state-

Justice Cooper, flood-bound at Toowoomba
(Q.) wired Judge Harding (Brisbane) to adjourn
the Full Court from day to day until Cooper’s
arrival. Whereupon the amiable Harding in open
court; “If his Honor had his books with him he
would find that what does not constitute a court
cannot adjourn a court.”
Maurice Brodzky, the journalist now in
the calcium glare over the mace case, turned up in
Melbourne about 18 years ago as a teacher of lan-
guages, in which he is a sort of Mezzofanti, and
exulted over rumours that he was a Russian spy,
being a Pole. He served, as a French Conscript,
in the Franco-German war.
When The Bulletin’s friend, “ Captain ”
Taylor, of land-boom and mace celebrity, applied
for admission as a barrister 22 years ago, Judge
Barry said to the “mover:” “1 cannot see the
applicant, where is he?” “ This is the applicant,”
replied the mover, “ Oh, that—er—person in the
light check suit and blue necktie ? ”
The trustees of Melbourne Public Library
ukase that henceforth no portraits of Australian
personages shall adorn the Art Gallery walls
unless the originals have become “eminent” or
“distinguished.” Shouldn’t some of those now
hanging be consigned to the dust-box ? That of
George Milner Stephen, for instance.
Mrs. Gibson, aged 60 years, the other day
completed an overlanding trip of 1085 miles, from
Mackay (Q.) to Nullamanna, near Inverell
(N.S.W.), in a one-horse buggy, and attended
only by a dog in addition to the horse. She
camped out regularly, with the exception of two
or three nights’accommodation at wayside houses.
Dr. Slade, the old-time spiritualist, who,
in Australia and elsewhere, made many thousands
out of his messages from deceased relatives, until
it was found that he had the slate with the spirit-
message on it planted under the table in advance,
is still alive. The other day an aged, penniless
lunatic was run in at Sioux city, lowa (U.S.), and
on examination lie proved to be Slade.

Called by a Bulletin paragraph to a
sense of its shocking neglect in respect to news-
paper-boss Bonython’s name on Lindsay’s explora-
tion maps, the S.A. Geographical Society has
stopped their distribution, and has issued a
second sheet. On this there is a Carclew Range,
called after the Bonython estate in Cornwall, a
Bonython Bluff, arid a Bonython Creek. Ye
gods !
Archbishop Carr, of Melbourne, who is
now a “ bigoted teetotaller,” drank wine when he
came to Melbourne in ’B7, but the number of
pubs, in his diocese and the hugeness of the
people’s capacity for grog so appalled him that he
took the pledge from his chaplain. When the
Archbishop now entertains his clergy at dinner
there is no fluid on the table stronger than soda-
water.
J. T. Harcourfc, once M.L.A. for Rich-
mond, and for many years proprietor of Cremorne
Lunatic Asylum, Melbourne, died last week, aged
80. He was a very red-faced and pompous old
gentleman, of most benevolent instincts, a
Wesleyan by faith, and he did not at all like to be
reminded that his insane establishment was con-
ducted on the site of the once giddy Cremorne
Gardens.
That respectable ex-savage, King George
of Tonga, died on the 19th February, aged 90
years. He was a profoundly thick-heaih d, but, so
far as can be judged, a well-meaning bigot, and
head of the Free Church of Tonga. In a general
way he was a duplicate of George 111., of Britain,
more especially in his hog-like capacity for rush-
ing down a steep place into the sea with the
Scripture under his arm.

“General” Pierre Gustave Toutant Beau-
regard, of the old Confederate army, died last
week, aged 75. He was a small and beautiful
dude, and his real name was Toutant—which is
very like Toots—only he called himself Beau-
regard, because it sounded better. Ho was a
most accomplished engineer, and when the war
was over, he spent his old age in engineering an
enormous sweep in New Orleans.
Perhaps the shortest Australian Acting-
Governorship on record was that of the late Judge
Redmond Barry. In 1875, when Gov. Bowen
(Vic.) got 12 months’ leave, Chief Justice Stawell,
then in England, almost “ broke Ins neck ” to get
back to reign at Government House. Bowen,
however, left for England three days ere StaweH’s
arrival, and as senior puisne. Judge the Grandi-
sonian Barry was Acting-Governor for 72 hours.
Fred. Want, the well-known attorney, late
of Sydney, was leaving a theatre in Shaftesbury-
avenue, London, when a fellow made a grab at his
watch-chain and broke it. Leaving .his wife with
a friend, Want gave chase, followed his quarry
through a labyrinth of streets, ran him to earth in
the Seven Dials, gave him an unmerciful punch-
ing, and then handed him over to the police. By
anyone who knows the locality the performance
will be appreciated.
Little detective Lomax, so much to the
fore in the Victorian mace scandal enquiry, has
been ever reputed the closest, discreetest, most
Star-Chambersome, mysterious, mistake-avoiding
member of the Victorian force—in a word, just
the man to put his foot into his month at last and
fall heavily. Lomax’s best feat at Melbourne
G.P.O. was nobbling an ex-Postmaster-General
of a neighbouring province, and getting him fined
£2, with costs, for writing inside a newspaper to
save a penny.
Hogan, M.P. (N.S.W.), was, in earlier
life, a policeman. Soon after election he called at
the department of a Minister —who also had been
a “trap”—on “roads and bridges” business. He
sent in his name, but the Minister was attending
a Cabinet meeting, and Hogan cooled his heels in
the passage for two hours. At length, gaining
admission, he said : “Look here, ; what
the divil do you mane by lavin’ me all this time
stuck up agin the wall outside your dhure, like a
blessed proclamation ?”
In the R.C. portion of Morpeth (N.S.W.)
grave-yard is a gravestone inscribed : “Of your
charity pray for the soul of Patrick G ——, who
left for his home in 1890,” Paddy is still very
much alive. Years ago he feared he was about to
die, and having little faith in human nature trans-
acted all business relating to the funeral obsequies
even to ordering his coffin, which he still has in
his possession. Even now Paddy’s stentorian
voice may be heard a pitch above all the rest in
his responses at the local R.C. Church.
Millionaire Burdekin, M.P., the great
bricks-and-mortar man, of Sydney, anathematises
the ■ prospective N.S.W. income-tax —comically
enough, in the interests of the “ working classes.”
But Burdekin has many good points. He is a
most generous landlord, and, though he has hun-
dreds of tenants, never “sells off’’anybody for
rent. If a man gets too heavily into his debt,
Burdekin’s agent simply says : “Pay a week’s
rent and let us have the premises.” Nor does he
ever raise a tenant’s rent; he waits until a new
tenant comes to increase the rent to the current
rate. For which reasons no man ever born could
beat the millionaire at an election for East Syd-
ney, which contains most of the big Burdekin
estate.
Shiels’ fiery orations always seem to be
thrown off on the spur of the moment, but in
reality he wrote them out in advance —just as
G. 11. Greed writes his best oratorical efforts —and
learned them with such awful accuracy that if one
epithet were missed in the report of a three hours’
deliverance he always observed the mistake and
corrected it in the proof. Patterson also writes
out his speeches, but, unlike Shiels, he cannot
learn them, though he tries terribly bard. He
sends a copy to the papers in advance, and then
he climbs on the platform and delivers quite a
different speech, and snorts around afterwards
because the reports don’t agree. The written
oration is laboured and painfully accurate Eng-
lish, and the spoken one sounds like a Chinese
riot in Lilly Boulke-stleet, and yet Patterson
firmly believes the two are the same.

“ Charley” Taylor, President of the Mel-
bourne mace inquiry, is a large, fat person with a
bass voice. During the old red sandstone epoch
of his now jet black inonstaehios the “capting”
(amateur) was a democrat of the most aggressive
character, but, perhaps, finding this brand of
politics unacceptable to the aristocratic denizens
of Hawthorn, he veered around until he was
landed in the Assembly. He now advocates the
vote for Theft, and refers to The Bulletin as
“a highly disreputable foreign paper.” Why the
swashbuckler with the “restored ” fringe beneath
his nose should call an adjoining province a foreign
country while he howls for Imperial Federation
and swears to mangle the enemies of a small island
off the coast of France, Heaven only knows. Any-
how, The Bulletin feels proud when it hears
itself characterised as disreputable by a person
like Taylor ; to be praised by that right-thinking
blusterer would be tew mutch.

Editor Bulletin, —In your issue of the
18th February is a paragraph to the effect that
Premier Griffith subscribed .£25 to the Queens-
land Flood Relief Funl, an amount, you went on
to say, which bore an insignificant proportion to
the sum “ earned for him by Mr. Jodrell ” in con-
nection with the Robb case, in his absence at the
Federal Council in Hobart. Will you allow me
to say—(l) that Premier Griffith subscribed £lOO
to the Flood Fund immediately on his return to
Brisbane; and (2) that not one penny was earned
for him by Mr. Jodrell or anyone else'in respect
of the Robb case, inasmuch -as Premier Griffith
did not receive, and never intended to receive any
fees in connection with the arbitration case during
the time he was away from Queensland. —Your
obedient servant, Joshua T. Bell, Brisbane, 20th
Feb., 1893.
A Melbourne contributor to The Bulle-
tin lately compiled a book of Higinbothamiana,
and took it to a leading firm of booksellers in the
forlorn hope that they would accept it for publi-
cation. Let him tell his story : —“ I saw a ferret-
faced Scot who looked at the title, then at me,
and said shortly, ‘ Why Democrat?’ ‘Because,’
I explained, ‘ Higinbotham was a Democrat above
all from first to last.’ ‘ I don’t think so," returned
the bookseller. ‘Oh, don’t you?’ said I. ‘Cer-
tainly not!’ he snapped. I then explained that if
he published on political principles we could do no
business. He then took another tack. ‘Does Pro-
fessor Morris know you are doing this?’ he asked.
I explained that as all the material was drawn
from official sources, I had thought it utterly un-
necessary to consult any of the late Judge’s con-
nections. ‘Oh,’ said the bookseller, ‘before we
can even consider the MS. you will have to ask
tlie Professor’s permission ; he is such a good cus-
tomer that I would not dream of doing anything
to offend him. Besides, he is bringing out a
memoir himself.’ I then simply remarked, ‘Good-
day.’ You now know, if you didn’t know before,
that that firm publishes strictly on Tory prin-
ciples. What earthly hope can there be for Pro-
fessor Morris’ memoir now ? ”
“I am Entirely Cured.”
Mrs. J. Jones, Burwood Junction, who writes on
January 12, 1893 : —I have been a great sufferer
from general debility, extreme weakness, and
nearly entire loss of sight. I was one year and
five months in Maitland Hospital, also nine
months in Morecliff Hospital. 1 was discharged
from both of them by the visiting doctors as in-
curable. I then went to the Newcastle Hospital,
and on being examined by three doctors, they
told me my case was hopeless, and they could do
no good for me, advising me to go to Sydney. I
was not in a position to do so. I was then taken
home quite disheartened. As I had tried a bottle
of Clements Tonic before when I was suffering
from influenza, and being cured of the above com-
plaint before I bad finished the bottle, my hus-
band advised me to give Clements Tonic another
trial. I did so, and, wonderful to say, instead of
my case being incurable, by the time I had taken
seven bottles of Clements Tonic I became a new
being, and now can certify that I am entirely
cured of my complaint. I have frequently gone
down on my knees and thanked God for this
wonderful cure. I can recommend it to every-
body who is suffering, and intend to do so, as I
can either speak or write it.—Yours gratefully,
Mrs. J. J ones, Melvilie-street, Burwood Junction,
N.S.W. . 1-
Signed on behalf of my wife to certify to the
above.— John Jones.

S.A. labour prospects at the coming elec-

rected. With ordinary luck, there will be ten
direct labour representatives elected in April.
One member of N.S. Wales Assembly,
“Colonel” Holborrow, is heard once a year and
no more. He annually asks : “ Will there be an
Easter encampment of volunteers this year ?”
The N.S.W. Labour-party’s proper course
is quite clear : to thwart the National Association
and the daily press, which support plural voting,
abolition of payment of members —and a dissolu-
tion.

sufficient for their support are exempted from

rest are bound to contribute proportionately to
their fortunes. ” —Robespierre.
Mr. Sargeant, M.P. (Vic.), got up in the
House, the other night, to express his utter abhor-
rence of the name Croajingolong, and to assert
his opinion that the county should be rechristened
and called after Prince George.
The S.A. public debt is £19,247,900, and
the yearly bill for interest £770,000 —whereof
public works return £025,000 annually. Only
£145,000 (or 9s. per head of population) has to be
made up out of general revenue.

brand, attends Q. Cabinet meetings and
advises as to the conduct of the elections.
The small fry, £l5O a year brand, are dismissed if
seen walking with a labour member.

Minister Copeland (N.S.W.) talks of “a
publican’s lawful avocation.” Dozens of people
blunder likewise. A publican’s vocation is to sell
liquor ; his avocation, or diversion from his lawful
calling, is usually in the direction of race-going.

new-carpet policy by ordering a lift up to his office

The Dibbs Government excuses itself
for not appointing labour members to N.S.W.
Upper House by saying that the names of all the
candidates for selection (including those_ of two
labour men) were put in a hat and the nine first
drawn were appointed. Unfortunately, the labour
names remained obstinately at the bottom.

so far apparently of undoubted status and integrity,
that the other day he mentally passed in review all
his friends and acquaintances, and concluded that
they were all more or less rogues —not one of them
was worth trusting. This is pretty well how Lhe
Bulletin feels about the Australian politicians.
A Hindoo correspondent protests, in a
Sydney paper, against the proposed exclusion of
Indians from Australia. “They are,” he says,
“by no means low in intellectual or moral develop-
ment, which is evidenced by their magnificent and
extensive literature and scientific culture previous
to any European group civilised.” But then look
at their legs !
It is just this way : N.S. Wales, after 40
years’ misgovernment by the hungry gang whose
loud-resounding shrieks for office and plunder are
thrown off their empty chests, is without man-
hood suffrage, the bedrock of democracy, and
without municipal government, a privilege long
enjoyed by Victoria, Queensland, South Australia,
and Maoriland.
Says Premier Dibbs, referring to the
inmates of the Old Men’s Asylum at Parramatta:
“ Many of the men are practically duplicates in
appearance of several members of the Assembly,
and some of them have magnificent heads.” The
way Dibbs discriminates between the men who
are duplicates and the men who have magnificent
heads is significant.
It becomes plain to The Bullletin that
the idea of the N.S.W. Legislative Council, in
seeking to tack on to the Electoral Bill a clause
abolishing payment of members, is to render it
strategically impossible for the Government to
tentatively accept the measure of reform already
assented to by the Council — i.e., the reduction of
the briok-and-mortar vote to a maximum of two
votes a man.
FREQUENT POINTS OF ATTACK :
The stomach, the liver, and the bowels, Wolfe’s
Schnapps by its stimulative glow strengthens
these and so prevents hurt.
Governor Jersey, or his locum tenens , no
more dare dissolve N.S.W. Parliament without
the consent of his Ministers than he dare hang a
convicted murderer against their advice.
London Times sneers at Australian attempts
at retrenchment as only heralding new loans.
Canada and the Cape, it says, issue per cent,
loans, like Victoria and N.S.W. The Canadian
stock stands at 104, the Cape stock at 101, the
N.S.W. at 96, and the Victorian at 89, “which
figures represent the operation of laws as inex-
orable and as impersonal as those which govern
the barometer.”
It is necessary that the men ofJS.S.W.
should govern themselves by means of pure man-
hood suffrage; also that the various communities
of the country should govern themselves under a
local government system. To secure these half-
century-delayed ends, now within reach, the
people should be ready to temporarily endure even
a Government run by Messrs. Nero, Parkes, Alfred
Allen, Caligula, and John Davies.
Last year in N.S.W. the increase in lunacy,
178, was unprecedented. The largest previous
increase in one year was 128, and the average
increase for ten years past was only 91. In other
words, the people of N.S.W. have started to go
mad nearly twice as fast as they used to do.
Among all white races the Irish have the largest
lunacy record, and the Spaniards the lowest. The
Australians stand about half-way between the
two, but are steadily “progressing.”
Let not “the great N.S.W. Freetrade
party ” jibe at millionaire Burdekin in his income-
tax agony. To the great Freetrade party Burdekin
has been both father and mother. He has also
been its milch-cow, and has fed it and clothed it;
has nursed it when it was sick, and lent it clothes
and jewellery when it was going to walk abroad.
He has paid its election expenses, and for the
washing of its dirty linen, and has many a time
and oft taken the bailiffs out of its house.

The same old cablegram “ The Orange
men of Belfast are highly indignant at Mr. Glad-
stone’s Horne Rule Bill, and_ declare that should
it become law they will resist it until death. ’ it
is interesting to find the peaceful Irish Unionist—
the individual who howls in so mournful a key
about the insurrectionary men of Tipperary—thus
announcing that he will start a big civil war as
soon as his miserable little minorityJfor he is in a
minority even in Ulster itself!) ceases to be
uppermost.
According to the cable, “Mr. Holmes,”
whoever he may be, proposes that the Austra-
lian colonies should assign their Customs duties as
security for their public debts.” Somebody ought
to cable back to this innocent child of nature, and
urge him to go to bed. Who is Holmes? The
cable is always breaking out with a bald state-
ment to the effect that Mr. Smith, of England,
thinks Australia should do something, or Mr.
Jones, of Great Britain, is of opinion it ought not
to do something. Who are all these people
Under the heading, “ Australia’s Oppor-
tunity,” an Argus correspondent writes gleefully
of the results which may ensue from an American
anti-immigration law. The law once passed,
hundreds of thousands of Polish Jews, Italians,
and desirable beggars of all nationalities, would
flock to Australia every year, and “ in ten years
make it the most prosperous country in the world.
Therefore, the Argus man exhorts every reader
of that broad-minded journal to pray for Austra-
lia’s opportunity to spring forth like—well, like a.
maggot from the carcase of “ America’s blunder.
Henry Wrixon’s agitation for a Govern-
ment statue to the late Justice Higinbotham sets
one thinking of the time when Higinbotham was
officially deprived of ins right to rank as Acting-
Governor in posse. Wrixon was Attorney-General
in the Ministry that worked the gross job. Now,
we have a definite clue to Wrixon’s sentiments on
the vice-regal system. He considers, no doubt,
that the personal character and intellect which
unfit a man for gubernatorial functions, qualify
him for the unique honour of a statue erected by
grant from the national exchequer. Is the gilded
lawyer listening ?
The N.S. W. Ministers always defend land
alienation on the ground that every man likes to
own his own home. They mean his own house,
and land alienation makes that impossible to 99
per cent, of the'eommunity. But in their abortive
Income Tax Assessment Bill they made it cheaper
fora man to live in any house other than his own.
The owner of a £400" house they would tax .£2O
annually on the capital value, while if he let
his house for £4O, and hired another with the cash,
he would escape all direct taxation, unless his in-
come were over .£2OO, when he’d suffer to the
extent of 4d in the £ only.

The Chinese invasion continues, says a
Westralian paper. Twenty-six coolies were landed
at Fremantle from the s.s. Astralind : Mr. E.
Roberts, Yatheroo, one gardener, one cook ; Mr.
W. Strickland, Perth, one waiter ; Mr. E. Keane,
Perth, one cook and one general servant; Weld
Club, Perth, two general servants; Ah Hong,
greengrocer, &c., Fremantle, six gardeners ; See
Shing, greengrocer, &c., Fremantle, two gardeners;
See Sing Woh, greengrocer, &e., Fremantle, four
gardeners ; Ah Sue, greengrocer, &e., Fremantle,
three gardeners; Ah You & Co., greengrocers,
&c., Fremantle, four gardeners.
A great scandal has been caused in Ade-
laide by the gift of .£O5O by Government to Mrs.
T. K. Pater, widow of the late local P.M., who
had only been in the service for half-a-dozen
years, during the greater part of which time he
received £lOOO per annum, or about double the
sum made by him in private practice. His widow
has only one child, and is by no means destitute.
Retrenchment is the rule in every branch of the
Civil Service, and many a poor clerk has been
docked to make up the amount named. The gift
and the circumstances attaching to it will be
remembered against the Government at the polls.
There are seven platitudes infesting the
pages of Hansard which ought to be abolished.
(1) That “the time is not yet ripe;” (2) that
“valuable interests are at stake;” (3) that “I
have a painful (or sacred and solemn) duty to per-
form ; ” (4) that “ the Government has borrowed
this measure from the Opposition programme; ”
(5) that “ I (Parkes) have occupied a seat in this
House for 30 years;” (6) that “the occasion is
inopportune,” and (7) that “law’n’order must be
maintained.” If any member using any one of
these seven remarks could be promptly fined £lO
business would progress a lot faster in Australia.

when his voting power is based on manhood
alone. The property-owner, however, can select
his electorate. Now, in these cheap days of photo-
graphy, what is to prevent each elector having a
printed right supplied him—with his photo, affixed,
all voters to be compelled to produce these when
recording their votes, said rights to be impounded
till elections are over ? This would be a pre-
cautionary measure against duplication or personi-

Young and old, short and tall, say G N tt N
Darlings the best of all.
Baker’s patent boundary-rider’s knife (W
Jas, Baker, cutler, &c., 3 Hunter-street, Sydney,
patentee) is an invention which must prove of the
utmost value to bushmen in general. It is a
strong and handy knife, fitted for a very wide
range of work. In the small sheath is a steel for
sharpening purposes, which convenience will be
much appreciated by the man who lives in lone-
some regions where the grindstone is not. In the
north-west especially one may travel many miles
without finding even a pebble, and under such
conditions the steel becomes an absolute necessity.
The price is moderate, and the knife is calculated
to outlast the owner by many years. For kan-
garoo hunters, and for skinning sheep and wild
animals, the combination of knife and steel will
be found invaluable.

Sarsaparilla. It is perfectly safe to take, and is a
powerful blood-purifier.

Society
A branch manager of a Melbourne bank
was recently told that, owing to bad times, his
services would be forthwith dispensed with. He
wrote to the directors saying he thought that after
20 years in the bank he was entitled to some con-
sideration. A reply came : “We run a bank, not
a charitable institution.” He has now resigned
his membership of the bogus Patriotic League,
and is daily expected to become an anarchist. As
Grant Allen says : “Hunger is a great teacher of
advanced political economy to men.”
A childless lady who wished
to adopt a baby lately advertised
in a Sydney evening paper:—“ Lady
wishes to adopt baby.” The clerk
who took the advt. said, “25.,
please! we have increased oureharge
for this sort of advertisement.”
Next day 13 answers were received,
and the same clerk, as he handed
them across the counter, remarked,
“ There will be as many more to-
morrow.” These 13 answers com-
prised 12 from women and one
from a man. This was written on
good paper in a good business
hand: “Re your advertisement,
will you kindly inform me as to
your terms and give me full particu-
lars, G.P.0.” One woman, signing
herself “Mrs.,” wrote ; “ Will yon
please call at No. , -street.”
Of the 11 remaining women six
signed themselves “ Mrs.,” three
“Miss,” and two signed in a
female Christian name; and the
11 letters had one common charac-
teristic —a desire expressed or im-
plied to get rid of an infant as soon
as possible. “lam a poor woman,
expect to be confined any moment,
and would like kind lady to take
my baby. If you will kindly reply
to Another : “I would like
to know if you would be willing
to adopt mine, it being a bright
little boy ; he is a healthy child, and
I would like to get someone to take
him. Trusting you will take him
and let me know as soon as possible.
P.S. —Urgent.” In no case was the
advertiser’s character, creed or social
position made a subject for con-
sideration. So much for parental
love. Four letters were perfumed,
four execrably written—apparently
by women who worked hard and
seldom wrote. In one instance the
handwriting might be a mere
child’s —a slow, laboured school-
hand. The letter was: “ I have
been seeking for someone to adopt
my baby. She is three months
old to-day. I should like someone
to adopt her for life. She is a very
good, healthy little thing. Please
oblige by replying directly.” One
application was peculiar : “I wish
to offer a thorough respectable baby-
girl to you by respectable parents.
I should wish to see you very much.
I await a reply. Call at once.
Parents and baby to be seen for
inspection.”
David Gaunson was supposed
to attend the mace -trial on behalf
of an engineer accused of abstract-
ing the bauble. When Brodzky
related the story he obtained from
Lomax, a circumstantial narrative
which, if true, quite established the
innocence of the engineer afore-
said, Gaunson chipped in with the
enquiry, “Was Mr. Brodzky sober?”
To the illegal mind this uncalled-
for suggestion seemed not to benefit
David Gaunson’s client any more
than the chairman’s assumption
that Lomax was mad. In fact, the
effect of legal training on both
Charles Taylor and David Gaun-
son, judging from their display in
this case, tempts one to suppose
that the next enquiry into Parliamentary scandal
might as well be conducted by unbiassed bottle-
gatherers,
# # •
The missing mace inquiry has already
brought two men to serious grief. Mr.
Maurice Brodzky, of Table Talk, first of all got
into much trouble with the committee (whose
chairman was good enough to refer to The
Bulletin as “a disreputable foreign paper”) by
refusing to give the name of the person who sup-
plied him with certain information which appeared
in his paper, and, after a bloodthirsty scene, was
fined .£2O. Then, being scared at the loss of his
monish, he suddenly caved in,- and indicated
Detective Lomax as his informant. In The
Bulletin’s opinion, Brodzky ought to have stood
his ground, like the Christian and Hebrew martyrs
who got their teeth pulled out in days of old,
1 a .her than go back on his informant, and either
paid his fine or else told the committee he would
see them darned first. Anyhow, he has lost a
glorious advertisement for his paper either way.
As for the detective, he will probably lose his
billet, if the “best people” can manage it, and
what this paper now wants to know is, whether
the curse of Deeming had anything to do with the
business? Did Lomax take any part in getting
up the case against that famous criminal?

One of the most impressive features of the
enquiry into the disappearance from the Victorian
Speaker’s-room of Matthew Davies’ gorgeous
silver Parliamentary mace was the bagnio-mistress
who dashed up to the chief entrance of Parliament
House in a giddy phaeton drawn by a pair of
bounding ponies, and waddled up the wide
steps with all the confidence of an old Parlia-
mentary hand—which, perhaps, she was. The
board’s eyes glistened, and they sat far back
when this mountain of buttercup embonpoint
appeared, and they vied with each other for the
pleasure of tenderly flipping a query into her large
salmon-coloured ears. “ There is decidedly NOT
any truth in the rumours that the mace was ever
in my house in Lonsdale-street. I never saw the
article in my house. [David Gaunson, appearing
d
pH I
fotr- (oul'ofa feiiiel' J

fur the engineer, here went off into convulsed con-
tortions.] I saw a picture of it once in the ’Ome
News, and often wished to see the real mace, but
the rumours are NOT true.” Then a poor old
deaf charwoman was called, by way of contrast;
some more people who could not possibly know
anything were seriously questioned as to their
knowledge, and the enquiry went downstairs to
vote for retrenchment.

Dear Bulletin, —Representing a local syn-
dicate of missing-worders, I am instructed to
enquire whether this imported London game is
played on a rational system, or whether it is
merely a mad, go-as-you-please entertainment.
You see, we appointed a board of learned men to
report on “ cupidity,” and the report is distinctly
unfavourable. The board is of opinion that
George Giffen couldn’t be cnpid on behalf, or in
the interests, of his young brother. The professor
in the chair went so far as to say that your miss-
ing word might as well have been “onions,” or
“bunions,” especially the latter. —Yours, _ Mel-
bourne. [This objection has something in it ;
still the - fact remains that 47 people named the
word, to say nothing of several successful and
very many unsuccessful competitors whose con-
tributions arrived too late, and will be returned,
less postage.]
A Sydney man won £'loo at 20 to I—laid
a month ago—that the new Governor of N.S.W.
would not be a commoner.
It would evidently pay the big ocean-line
steam-companies to hire a few titled people to
travel outwards from Australia by every steamer.

The action of Sydney Hospital Committee
in appointing as president the absent title-hunting
Dr. Ren wick (who would seem from this to be
hunting titles at both ends of the world at the
same time), instead of having for its chief execu-
tive officer a man on the spot, is most reprehen-
sible, especially considering the present abject
state of-the institution’s finances. Likewise, the
committee’s action in handing over a ,£3OOO or so
contract to the firm of one of its own members
without competition looks a very fishy proceeding.
And what can be said of the smother-it-up action
which led to .£7O, paid for the shorthand-reporting
of the secret enquiry re the doctors’ squabble, being
charged as “stationery?” We hear that the
records of that disgraceful row have been destroyed
in the interests of the medical profession.

A teacher of a Southern N.S.W. State-
school flogged the son of a German ex-soldier, who
appeared next day carrying a drawn sword, with
which he attempted murder. The athletic teacher
beat down the German’s guard with a long map-
hook, took the sword, demanded and 'received an
abject apology, and now has the blade as a trophy
in his drawing-room.
Governor Jersey takes back to England 2C
of the servants he brought out.

Out of the 50 and odd divorce-suits now
being heard in Sydney (N.S.W.), there is only one
case set down for titillating the palates of the
jury. Judge Windeyer will banquet solus on the
remainder. One couple, one divorce. The deser-
tion clause of the Act—absence for three years and
upwards—is being largely availed of. The public
ear is itching for the marvellously sensational
details of M‘Donald v. McDonald.
* * *
N.S.W. has at least one sensible and prac-
tical Quarter Sessions Judge. At Orange, the
other day, a man was indicted for having declared
that his illegitimate child was legitimate. He
said that he swore falsely in order to shield the
mother, and added : “ I’m ready to marry the girl
now.” The Court was adjourned for his" imme-
diate wedding, which was ceremoniously perpe-
trated in the gaoler’s house. The bridegroom was
then discharged, an old boot, scientifically ad-
ministered, helping to crown him with luck.
* * *
New : “So they were married, and lived
happily never after.”

The death of the bright and amiable Mrs.
Alexander Cameron, of Sydney, which took place
last week after a short illness, has much grieved
a wide circle of friends. The deceased lady, who
was not yet 30 years of age, was the daughter of
Mr. Kahlo, late XJ.S. Consul for N.S.W., and had
been married but a year or two.
# * *
At Manly (N.S. W.), in the summer time,
many young ladies go out very early in the morn-
ing to have a swim in the ocean. A clerical-
looking person in a black suit and a white choker
and a broad-brimmed soft hat also goes out early,
and wanders down to the beach with a book—pre-
sumable a work of pious meditation—in his hand,
and a big umbrella under his arm. Arrived at
the beach he sits down opposite the female con-
tingent in the surf, unfurls the volume on his
knee, opens the umbrella, and apparently plunges
into an ecstasy of holy thought. It has lately
been discovered, however, that there is a hole in
the umbrella, and that the godly man glares through
it with an opera-glass at the ladies in the. water.
Therefore he is hereby advised that the next time
a certain girl—aged 16, and apparently an object
of much interest to the meditative expounder-
goes down to bathe, her uncle and guardian in-
tends following behind, and when he gets within
range proposes to bounce a big rock off the
umbrella and afterwards to kick the preacher
with much violence.

“Freedom of contract” resulted in the

“ Free labour” is tbe scum of the earth ; there is

On Tuesday, Feb. 21, Captain Lockyer, of
the steamer Innamincka, completed a service of
20 years with the Adelaide S.S. Co., during which
time he has carried 171,000 passengers absolutely
without mishap.

And thought the thought I dared not speak—

Wi. h all the pool—for really, now,
Such swindles have occurred —
O ! Shan’t we have a row I”
Now find the missing word.
He did not seize the bag and fly
With all the stamps we sent;
But, said, “ I’ll pay ‘ Cupidity ! ’ ”
{Stupidity he meant).
The finished sentence lost all force,
Its ending was absurd ;
Next time I'll back a—— horse —
You find the missing word.
Experience and cuteness go
For something when yon “ punt,”
But here’s a game where what you know
Will keep you from the front.
Cupidity ! Boss, we pro’est!
Let payment he deferred.
O, ! I wish I hadn’t guessed—
Now find the missing word.

year total .£440 per annum. The iMOO goes to

One Sydney bookseller has sold no less
than 750 copies of “ Teas of the D’Urbervilles.”
The question of nastiness lately raised in con-
nection with the book by certain of the pious
prurients, who ever revel in discussions of this sort,
has raised the Australian sale by leaps and bounds.

“The Times” recalls an episode that
occurred in a wealthy Sydney family. The trusted
and comely Bridget, who had served her mistress
faithfully for two years, gave a week’s notice.
“Very well, Bridget! I’ll write yon a good refer-
ence.” “1 don’t want one, mum!” “But,
Bridget, you will want it.” “No, muni, if they
ask me whoy Oi left me last place Oi’ll tell ’em
bekase the back stairs and kitchen flare wasn’t
put out to wash, and if they ask me whoy Oi left
you O’U ask them whoy their last maid left them.
You may look, mum, but Oi’min very high spirits
—Oi’m to be married, loike, this day wake.” The
family, who \vere all attached to Bridget, made
her many useful presents. The promising son of
the family went to see Bridget married, and he
did see her married —to himself.

Scene, a back-blocks Sunday-school. Good
old gentleman in frock-coat examining the class
in scriptural knowledge. Small girl, who had
been accidentally overlooked : “Please, Mr. ,
you haven’t asked me any question.” Good old
gentleman : “ Oh, I quite forgot you, ray little
girl. Well, how long was Moses in the wilder-
ness?” Little girl (scornfully): “Is that all?
Why, 40 days and 40 nights, of course. That’s up
against your duck-’house, ain’t it?”

Englishmen resident in the autocratically-
ruled Dutch East Indies have, for years past, had
to put up with the most outrageous insults from
the officials. Last year, at Batavia, Douglas
Archibald, the phonograph-man, had some experi-
ence of Dutch rule. Staying at the same_ hotel
was a very sickly little Englishman, a resident,
whose inability to retaliate made him the constant
target for the Dutchmen’s dinner-table insults.
Next to him always sat a burly Hollander, who
was his especial torment, and who once actually
asked him in a loud tone —“ What is the most
insulting thing I can say to an Englishman?”
This was told to the phonograph-man, who got
the sick boarder to change seats with him next day.
The table was pretty well crowded when the
BOATLESS.
SHE: “What a tremendous swell Smith-Jones has become! He
wouldn’t think of wearing a pair of black leather boots now;
they must be tanned.”
He: “His tastes have not altered much; I remember when he wore
tanned feet.”
visitor took his chair, and before the first course
was half-through, amenities commenced by the
Dutchman making a most insulting observation to
the new-comer —who in about two minutes bestowed
on him a face like a badly-designed patchwork
quilt. The phonograph-man was hurried off to
prison, where he discovered that the Dutch don’t,
as he had hopefully imagined, fine an Englishman
for a common assault. i They kept him in gaol for
a month, and then, with his luggage, he was taken
off to a British-India boat, sent out of the country,
and charged “ never to coom pack no mores.”

We have received No. 2 of the Modern
Review, which, like No. 1, is pious and dirty, and
has in it a portrait of a clergyman—a saint witli a
blubbery mouth and a sinister, leary eye, well-
adapted for looking up dark lanes late at night.
The contents consist of articles on marriage,
maternity, cancer, diseases in the British army—
which seems, by the description, to be in a very
diseased condition, hypnotic suggestion—the
Modern Review is always more or less suggestive
—woman, and a shriek headed “ Women—don’t
despair.” Also, there is a black and gruesome
tale about the horrible cruelties practised on
children in England as revealed by the Society for
the Prevention of Cruelty to Children—a tale
which makes one wonder at the gall of that Bull
who sends more missionaries to the heathen than
any other nation on this earth. In one of the
articles appears the statement that “ the subject
of sexual relationship is largely occupying the
public mind.’’ In this respect the Modern
Review is in the same position as the public mind.

Melbourne and suburban cemetery trustees
complain of large reductions in receipts. This,

Astronomer Ball mentions that if the news
of Waterloo had been telegraphed at once to the
star, A Centauri, it would not have arrived yet
though electricity travels several times round the
world in a second. There are other stars which
the telegraphed news of the Norman Conquest
would not yet have reached, and others again of
which the same may be said in regard to the
Crucifixion.
'# * •
In George - street, Sydney. A woman,
paralytically drunk, holds on to a lamp-post, and
listens to a blind man reading a chapter of the
Bible. “I ain’t (hie) got a blessed (hie) bean
left,” she snivelled, searching her pockets. A
benevolent old fellow, standing by, pleased to see
she evidently felt inclined to help the blind
reader, pressed a shilling into her hand. The story
ought to close with the woman dropping the
money into the blind man’s money-box, but it
doesn’t, for she pocketed the coin and made a zig-
zag line to the nearest whisky-mill.

Retrenchment on the absentee-owned
N.W. N.S.W. stations is being carried to a very
fine point. Most of the old-time popular managers
have been superseded by a new species, bred,
apparently, to the order of the shareholders and
matured in the strong-rooms of the Loan Co.’s
offices. In consequence, a day’s ride over the
dreary nor’-western plains is rendered more
depressing than ever by the now inevitable recep-
tion at the station ahead where the traveller will
be told that “there’s good picking for the horse
on the travelling stock route near Dingo Flat
or some such place where the station stock have
loafed for months. But one big Melbourne syndi-
cate have, in retrenching, hit a snag. Their vast
runs have for years past been under the general
control of a manager enjoying a fat salary who
had encouraged much land settlement on the Co. s
leased holdings. Recently a new manager came
up with a letter from the Co. enclosing to their old
servant many regret, high testimonials, a bonus
and -the sack. Taking the lot in the spirit it was
meant the ex-general manager handed over his
charge and proceeded to settle down at one home-
stead on secured land, telling his successor, when
asked for an explanation, that he was now going
to spell on his run. An investigation by the
now alarmed Board of Directors showed
that their late manager had, in his many transac-
tions with selectors, protected his employers from
all suspicion by having his name only inserted in
all mortgages and subsequent transfers, and as an
outcome of his zeal the Co. are now expecting to
have to pay him grazing-rates, whilst the manager
calmly waits for the Co. to open its mouth wide
enough to satisfy him. This card is said to be
held by several other hopelessly- involved run-
holders when the Loan Co.’s sci’ew is put on.

Five doctors, in a recent Liverpool (Eng.)
police-court case, refused to be sworn. The old
Italian proverb was “Five doctors, four infidels.'

“Jawbone” Neild, Freetrader, invented
a new variety of obstruction last week in N.S.W,
Assembly, when he gave notice of motion that the
Chairman of Committees (Melville) “no longei
possesses the confidence of the House.” This idea
is capable of indefinite expansion. There is nr
reason, for instance, why Wall should not move
that the House has lost faith in Torpy, or why
Tonkin shouldn’t get up a long debate on a motion
that Hogan doesn’t possess the confidence of the
Assembly, or why somebody shouldn’t move a
vote of censure on'the Refreshment Committee, or
its whisky, or the Standing Orders Committee,
or the doorkeeper, or anybody. What Neild
doesn’t know about obstruction doesn’t amount to
much.

world has ever seen for the immediate relief and

doctors, delighted sufferers, and dispelled the
doubts of the most incredulous. Its virtues are
praised by thousands all over the land, who have
been rescued from years of suffering and torture
with every species of disease, and granted a new
lease of health and activity. When physicians
have certified to their inability to dire rheumatism,
neuralgia, and other very painful disorders, by
giving up their patients to the stubborn claims of
protracted disease and possible death itself, the
St. Jacobs Oil, by its unpretentious merit, has
gained in every instance yet greater fame from its
almost magical influence over these diseases. This
remedy has no uncertain or miraculous history;
its decided power resides in a peculiar volatile oil,
carefully distilled from thePiNUS Svlvestrxs, and
has been used and distributed for hundreds of
years by the inhabitants of the Black Forest, a
mountainous and densely-wooded region in Baden
and Wurtemburg, Germany. The introduction of
the St. Jacobs Oil into Australia has been attended
with the same distinguished results as have ever
characterised its employment in America and
Europe ; and its adoption as the national remedy
for rheumatism, neuralgia, and all painful disease’s
is assured. When it is considered that this won-
derful pain-banisher and health-restorer can be
had at all drug-stores for such a small sum, it
appears as a matter of duty to every sufferer with
rheumatism, neuralgia, or kindred complaints to
seek the relief which St. Jacobs Oil cannot fail to
give. Try it and you will be convinced. It can
be obtained from all chemists and druggists.

The triple alliance, Russia, Germany»
Italy, is virtually ended, while France and Italy

Shearing-machines are not a perfect success
in Maoriland, as they clip the sheep too closely.
In Southland, during a slight frost on a recent
night, 4000 newly-shorn-sheep perished from cold,
owing to their skins being practically bare.
Gladstone’s weakest point is that he can’t
bear interruption. Disraeli was the only man who
could drive him to absolute frenzy. He used to
go to sleep when the G.O.M. was denouncing him,
or else he would yawn wearily, and rise up with a
bored, wooden expression and lounge out of the
House like a man lost in deep thought, and then
Gladstone would bound and prance with rage, and
heave _ adjectives and maledictions after his
departing enemy.
An Argus writer suggests that Australia
mop up the itinerant paupers now being excluded
from the U.S. “I appeal to the common-sense
of any old Australian, to say whether half-a-
million of people a year, doing exactly as the
early settlers did . . , would not in ten years
make Australia the most prosperous country in
the world,” But how are the imported paupers
going td play the land-grab game “exactly as the
early settlers did ? ” Will Tyson; or Wilson; or
Chirnside grant them a thousand acres of arable
land in exchange for mere residence, or for rum,
true religion and coloured blankets ?
Ministei? Lyne (N.S.W.) points out that
the late Parkes’ Government resumed land in one
year to the value of a million and a quarter sterl-
ing, which land doesn’t now yield the State even
one '"per cent, on the cost of resumption. At
Darling Harbour, =£loo,ooo was paid for land
which now produces annually .£7OO ; and .£29,000
was paid for land at Circular Quay which, as was
known at the time, could have been purchased a
few hours previously for £14,000. The Parkes
Government gave £275,000 for an old wharf at
Circular Quay, with the queerly coincidental
result that the shares held by Ms.P. supporting
[the Ministry instantly rose 100 per cent, in value
