Ist March, 1893.
My Dear Moouablnda
The sale of “effects” at Sydney Government
House last week was said to be a most successful
auction— everything fetched almost its full value.
Another of tlie many advantages of being a
Governor —vice regal cast-off chairs and tables
have more virtue for some people than the brand-
new articles fresh from the—er—Chinaman’s.
Apropos, I haven’t heard of any unthinking bids
at the Jersey sale, so that the Loftus silver tea-
pot is still the only dismal warning to the extra-
enthusiastic bidder at a sacrifice of vice-regal
house-gods.
After all the exciting rumours of Lord This and
That, with the further certain contingency of
Lady This or That, the end is plain Mr. and Mrs.
Duff! to “ govern ” N.S. Wales in place of the
Earl and Countess of Jersey. The “ Right Hon.
R. W. Duff, P.C.,’’ is certainly quite imposing
enough for death-warrants and so on, but conver-
sationally there will be no resource save a weary
iteration of “ Yerexcellency.” I wonder how a
Society thickly peppered with brand - new,
specially-manufactured “Sirs” and “Ladies”
will like the idea of worshipping at the shrine of
His Excellency Mr. Duff? “ Thank you, Missus
Duff,” says Lady De Pigsfoot, of Botany Bay, at
Govingment ’Ouse reception. “ Oh, don’t
mention it, your ladyship,” replies plain Mrs.
Duff, with deep, but wasted sarcasm.
Are hereditary titles shying at Australian
offices and emoluments? In N.S.W. the two
leading official positions are held by Misters, with
plain “Mrs.” to lead society in consequence.
Mrs. Duff and Mrs. Bowden-Smith are the local
social arbitresses awhile, right over the heads of
Lady Blank , and Lady So-and-so —proving the
value, of “colonial” titles even “within the
colony ! ”
No more enthusiastic audience ever assembled
than tiiat which greeted Gov. and Lady Jersey at
the farewell tendered them by the Sydney Boys’
Brigade, when Premier Dibbs formally handed
over the title-deeds of a site for the erection of a
much-needed lodging-house for boys. Lady
Jersey had a splendid reception and was given the
most attention—which speaks volumes for the in-
herent chivalry of the untaught little beings who
turned the affair into an election meeting when
the gentlemen were trying to speak. Her lady-
ship (livelier dressing than plain black and white
would have better adorned the occasion—boys are
notorious worshippers of lively clothing) made a
very neat speech. One really funny incident
occurred. A man was sitting down with his hat
tn. “Take yer ’at off !” shrieked a chorus of
little champions of etiquette. The victim took it
off reluctantly, revealing a very bald head, “ Oh,
Lord, put yer ’at on again ! ” shrieked the merci-
less Brigade.
Lady Jersey’s farewell “mob” reception in
Sydney Government House Gardens on Monday
was an omnibus function, ranging from Paris
frocking to a poor old lady with bare and soiled
hands, a shabby tartan skirt and a scrunched
bonnet, who seemed to me to be like the shade of
some forgotten ancestress of Potts Point appearing
in an “as I was so you won’t be ” sort of spirit.
Lady Jersey, supported by the Governor, stood
to her guns near the inner garden gate, and wore
a mottled bluish silk, with lines of pink flowers,
and trimmed with blue silk and cream lace, bonnet
of gold, galon lace and flowers. Her eldest
daughter sported blue French foule, with epau-
lette collar of white guipure lace, and a wide white
gauze hat.
Unquestionably the most modish “all together”
was the dress worn by Mrs. Burdekin—a black-
striped silk grenadine with sleeves of striped black
and green dotted with gold beads ; Empire sash
tied in the front, of black satin designed in red and
green wheat-ears; Paris hat of green velvet
trimmed with black ribbon and feathers. Mrs.
(Mayoress) Manning wore a very imposing turn-
out of black satin brocaded in violet and made
with huge puff sleeves, of violet velvet; small
bonnet cn suite with touches of green. Miss Bur-
dett-Smith wore black spotted grenadine and
black jet; picture hat smothered with black
feathers. Mrs. Pilcher wore black designed in
grey with zouave of white lace. Pretty Miss Pil-
cher looked very up-to-date in a Russian blouse of
blue spotted in white, jam-pot hat of green velvet
trimmed with crimson roses. Mrs. G. R. Dibbs
wore black satin with a lace visite, and pink rosea
in her bonnet; Miss Dibbs, green figured
pongee with cream silk vest; Miss Williamson
wore pink and green fould very effectively cut,
topped off by a Frenchy floral hat —in fact, the
dresses were gayer than the proceedings, which
were rather sad owing to there being no music to
engender Dutch conversation, as it were. Indeed,
the only amusing thing was the farm-yard-at-
feeding-time rush for the tea-tables set out on the
verandah. A lot of people were there —and a lot
of people did not attend. You see, the Jerseys
wer e going; they had not just come. And so it
was at the banquet to Gov, Jersey.
At the citizens’ farewell dinner, the Town Hall
was tricked out v.cli palms and flags. The cross
(or disciples’) tab! u , occupied by the notabilities,
were especially festive florally. The attendance was-
very good, although one or two clubs had dined
His Excellency beforehand and the Union was to
entertain him next evening. There was a good
muster of femininity up aloft. If only the great
organ hadn’t been played all through, as if the gifted
Wbgand wanted to hide a thunderstorm, the
farewell dinner to Lord Jersey would have been
charmingly stage-managed.
Mayoress Manning entertained a party of ladies
in the Northern gallery, including Lady Jersey
who looked very tired (it was in execrable taste
to force a speech from her in such a place),
and wore a gown of bright yellow satin brocade,
with folded bodice, berthe of cream lace, and
magnificent diamonds that glittered like stars.
Lady Margaret Villiers —seen in public for the
first time with her hair “done up” wore pink
silk with big Empire sleeves; berthe of pink
chiffon, and* like her mother, carried a beautiful
posy. Mrs, Manning wore white satin, covered
with black lace —full sleeves of black velvet;
diamond ornaments. Mrs. Martin (wearing
snappy diamonds), Mrs. Dibbs, Mrs. Burdekin
and Mrs. Frank Suttor wore black. Miss Mary
Martin relieved her black gown with yellow and
sunflowers ; Mrs. (Colonel) Mackenzie contrasted
black and green. The only odd dress I saw was a
bright red gaberdine-y gown, which made the
wearer look dreadfully like a stick of sealing-wax.
When the banquet was over, Mrs. Manning held
a small farewell reception to Lady Jersey, who
must indeed have been delighted with the oppor-
tunity to squeeze another hundred fists, after
spending the best part of the afternoon in pump-
handling the madding crowd.
The (theatrically) best - liked of Australian
Governors is the braw Kinlore, who makes him-
self tremendously agreeable at a show. He doesn’t
sit bolt upright, staring at the drop for fear he
will have to bow to somebody, but sends wreathed
smiles all around, and in the Intervals chats
freely and pleasantly—he even leaves the sacred
box to do it ! Consequently, V.R. night in Ade-
laide attracts the whole Government Ouse crowd
to the play, just to be seen hobnobbing with the
Governor in a public place. Gov. Kintore, in
fact, thoroughly pervades a theatre when once he
gets there.
I don’t know whether the Huffs will “do like-
wise,” but whenever the Jerseys patronised a
Sydney theatre most of the “ best people ” stayed
away. A V.R. circle in recent times sported more
cheap silk, over-perfuming, and hair-oil than any
other ten circles on ordinary occasions. I have
often had an idea that His Ex. bought up the
whole horseshoe and distributed the tickets every-
where so as to have to avoid the possibility of
having to know anybody on these frequent
occasions.
The election of Mrs. Lawson—mother of the
talented Henry Lawson, whom I always think of
as the Australian Burns —to Sydney School of
Arts committee (an aggregate of misogynists), is
hailed by the she-suffragists as another chance for
lovely woman to publicly assert herself. But, from
my standpoint, the fewer opportunities she gets
the better for her ultimate chance, if I may speak
paradoxically. The unkindest thing men can do,
it seems to me (I’ve just heard about the doings
and undoings of theN.S.W, ladies’ Chicago lair
Committee), is to give the dear souls the reins,
and then sit back and watch the fun.
A Sydney penlady, after asserting that women’s
meetings are as well-organised and as methodically
conducted and as free from devious wanderings as
men’s, draws attention to the well-known don’t-
oare-a-rap spirit of the average woman, even upon
questions relating to her own welfare. During
the local School of Arts excitement, despite
urgent invitations per circular, only 20 women
presented themselves to vote their sister in.
Which is quite in accord with all one’s ideas of
Woman, who is never remarkable for comradeship
except there’s a man in it. She is always ready
to talk nineteen to the dozen, but really does not
in such matters care a straw who is in or out, so
long as her new curling-pins work properly, and
her baby looks quaint in a Greenaway cap. She
is built like that proverbially. If women were all
creatures of big brains and small hearts—full of
logic, but empty of love (love and logic can never
go together, for there’s no logic in love)—well, it
wouldn’t take long before the world was to be let.
To put it straight, “Woman doesn’t want to
advance her sex”—she wants to advance the other
sex, and I don’t blame her. You naturally get
up more enthusiasm over the side which treats
you best—which gives you a seat in crowded
vehicles instead of spreading out skirts and
umbrellas and glaring at you as if you had horns ;
which adores you sometimes to supporting-point
and is generally agreeable, and which admires
your new hat without trying to see whether it
isn’t last year’s bonnet masquerading in a new
feather. I say it honestly and reverently—true
women hate one another like poison. However,
the fact is not at all discreditable. It’s only
another aspect of professional jealousy.
One word of advice to people about to buy frocks.
Plaid will have a good run this season, and the
N.S. W. girl who wants to cut a dash will hunt up
the Duff tartan if she intends to lather her gown
with violent checks. But the difficulty is to find
the tartan. I have already been shown two that
were “positively the Duff,” altho’ they were as
different from each other as tartans well could be.
By the way, the trouble about the bust of the
N.S.W. Chief Justice has bowled a certain lady
out in a lovely little diplomatlsm. The bust, she
said, was not despatched to familiarise the world
with the Chief Justice (who, the lawyers all say,
hasn’t a brain to match his Roman brow), but “as
an example of an Australian lady’s handiwork.
But as the lady who perpetrated it is only recently
from ’Ome, the spirit of George Washington evi-
dently deserted the ladies’ committee in a very
tight place.
Sydney Women’s College doesn’t seem to be
quite the one thing femininely needful. It is two
years since the fuss began, and the result is eight
students. Perhaps, however, when the College
building is up there will be a bigger rush, because
it would be far more interesting to cultivate your
mind in the “’Varsity” area than in a mere
furnished house near the tram-line. For one
thing, the University grounds are an excellent
place to show off a red umbrella. And then
there are the he-students, who are too remote
from the present Women’s College.
The recent shocking neglect of a dying Sydney
servant-girl was certainly one against the employ-
ing class, but there are balancing instances. A
medico was about to administer chloroform to a
lady before performing a critical operation, the
other day, and before taking the drug she asked if
her little children were safe with the maid. A
nurse was dispatched to see, and returned with the
news that both babies were playing in the street,
and that Miss Gwendoline O’Regan had just gone
for a music-lesson !
In vino veritas. The truth in wines is that
Innes’ Boar’s Head Australian wines have the
largest sale in Sydney. This is owing to their
excellent quality and low price, and it enables the
most economical to enjoy wines which, for purity,
flavour and aroma, are unequalled by the finest
vintages of the old world. The Boar s Head
brand comprises wines of all prices and qualities
—ports, clarets, muscats, hocks, and others too
numerous to specify.
There is an offioially-unauthenticated rumour of
the madness of a Gaiety songstress —the Marquis
of Queensberry admired her very and
travelled per same boat to Australia. She is said
to be now in Colney Hatch with straws in her
hair. Which recalls the fact that the great
warbler in question was never characterised by a
strictly normal deportment—although when an
actress is the case in point you never quite know
how much is in the method. At a theatrical picnic
down Sydney harbour ehe once turned up in a
trailing black velvet robe looped high on one side,
her hair hanging loose and skewered with a big
dagger, and her eye in a hne frenzy rolling, but
everybody thought it was j ust for effect. Gaiety
girls are so addicted to that. For instance, one
pretty girl came down to the Lyceum the other
evening with a parrot perched on her shoulder.
“I can’t get it off—pretty cocky,” she said to the
manager, “it stuck on all the way down the
street.”
When you aren’t quite as facially fascinating as
Adonis, and your wife can’t give points to Venus
—put up in your hall by way of foil a pair of
villainously ugly South Sea Island gods. This
prescription, which I find practically adopted by
a very eminent personage, can be strongly recom-
mended.
A Queensland politician who (outside his pro-
vince) is a man of flattering repute, still suffers
severely from the effects of having burst through
the roof of a room occupied by three Hebes—one
of whom received him with a blow on the head
from a leaden-backed hair-brush.
The true motive of the average philanthropist
comes out beautifully in the conscientiously “ cor-
rected ” Sydney subscription-lists for the poor
washed-put Bananalanders : “Erratum: for Miss
Snooks—one guinea—read Miss Nooks — one
guinea. For Mr. Asterisk —one guinea—read the
Rev. Dr. Asterisk—one guinea.” Picture the
calculating charity that insists upon a correction
if its middle name isn’t properly spelt! Why it
isn’t charity at all—it’s simply cheap advertising.
From Messrs. W. H. Paling & Co. I have
received, in a chastened spirit, that classic and
popular melody, “I’ve chucked up my Push for
the Donah.” Every copy contains a Sydney and
a Melbourne version. In the one, the lady is met
at Chowder—in the other, down the Bay, which
is the grand difference in the poetry of the thing.
Simple minds which essay the song will want the
help of a thieves* dictionary before singing it with
due empressement.
A very smartly-dressed crowd, headed by Lord
Jersey and his eldest son, greeted Miss Maud
Fitzstubbs (Mrs. Harry Woods) on the occasion
of her benefit concert in York-street Hall, last
Saturday. The stage was decorated with potted
palms, and all the performers were brightly
dressed—Mrs. Woods turning out very charmingly
in pink and silver. The Governor was entertained
during the interval by a society-solicitor, who
gave His Excellency a list of the best Japanese
hotels (let’s hope that was all) to soften the vice-
regal path through the land of umbrellas. As for
the music—the best items on a bill of thirteen
numbers were those with which was associated the
most easy and dexterous beneficiary.
Is there any other profession that carriesits
phraseology into private life as does the theatrical
profession '! In a Sydney draper’s shop, the other
day, a well-known actress was inspecting some
neckties. As it was rather dark at the counter
she said to the shopman ; “ If you wouldn’t mind
I’d like to see them from the front ! ” It was
strange she didn’t ask the shop-walker to light the
footlights instead.
Some people are most punctilious about having
things properly stated. The other day I received
an announcement of a coming marriage which ran,
“ Blank versus Dash. At St. So and So.’s,” &c.
Versus is very lovely.
I hear of a curate who was offered and accepted
a South Australian incumbency. On getting the
post he immediately wedded the star of his soul,
but on wiring the fact to his churchwardens in a
joyous outburst of confidence received a curt
reply stating that his marriage disqualified him,
as it was absolutely necessary the parson should
be single ! Thus do the deacons work in with the
Dorcases and keep the spiritual ball a-rolling.
Clergymen seem to be ticketed like a lot of goods
in a draper’s window! For instance, a recent
Sydney church scandal elicited the fact that if a
congregation pays, say, .£250 per annum for a
soul-saver, they’ve to take what’s given to them
by the bishop,—say, a damaged parson shaky on
one or two minor theologio points, or not well-
connected, and so on. But if they pay higher
they can choose their own prophet—have a trial of
his intonation and temper and make sure of his
ideas on a material hell. How funny !
Sad to see societies so full of hope and courage
and enthusiasm turn to utter listlessness when
once the titled impetus is gone. The last awful
example is the Queen’s Fund—it used to be the
“ Women’s Fund ’’—which held its last monthly
meeting without a title present, and only a hand-
ful of members. Should this continue, the
“ Queen’s ” Fund will have to go, despite the fact
that Lady Carington is still the president. When
a society which once was ultra-fashionable and
embarrassed even under the auspices of my Lady
This and That and the Other, has to hoist a plain
Missus into the chair, it won’t be long ere the
shutters go up with a bang.
The latest storyette about one of the prettiest
Gaiety girls of them all is that she and two ador-
ing Johnnies chased a frantic pussy through the
corridors of her hotel, and at last whacked it to
death in the bath-room—the yells of the wretched
cat, mingled with the laughter of its pursuers,
sounding so horrible that one or two women
fainted on hearing it. By the way, the spectacle
of a Gaiety girl committing a furious assault on a
tabby appears to me to have quite an allegorical
aspect.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
The unblushing effrontery of the crowd
which lias been lately endeavouring to work
up a public agitation for a dissolution of
N.S.W. Parliament is a thing that ought to be
pre erved in spirits and exhibited in a museum.
Parkes took the chair at the meeting which de-
manded that the Governor should dissolve the
Assembly against the advice of the Cabinet, yet
that same Parkes, in his half-ton book, expressly
declares that the Governor has no power to do
anything of the kind ! Ex-Treasurer M‘Millan
made a furious speech demanding a dissolution,
and when the Governor refused to grant it blandly
remarked that his Ex. was quite right. M‘Millan
also introduced the deputation, and then tried to
run, and had to be brought back to see his own
game out. Reid and Wise, who helped on the
movement and promised to join the deputation,
never turned up at all. The whole crowd was
utterly ashamed of the business, and the “ Rise of
the People ” dropped dead right there and hasn’l
been heard of since.
Schey’s Eight. Hours Bill lapsed for want
of a proposer and seconder in the N.S.W. Legis-
lative Council.
Under N.S.W. Treasurer See’s Income-
tax Bill, if a man receives an income of .£2OO only
he escapes taxation; if just over .£2OO, he pays
4d. in the £on the excess. But if, with 25 others,
he draws £2OO yearly by means of corporate effort,
his income is taxed up to lOd. in the £. A tax on
co-operation.

Statutory Declaration.
ITHOS. MU DIMAN, Master Tailor, of 515 George Street,
, Sydney, in the colony of New South Wales, do hereby
solemnly and sincerely declare as follows :
Whereas, I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the Nerves, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried several
Doctors and many Remedies, but all of no avail. Whereas,
1, as a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rassmnssea’s
PURELY HERBAL “ UNIVERSAL ALFALINE
FILLS,” which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare
COMPLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and
although this is over five years ago, I have remained well ever
since.
THE ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration, conscientiously believing the same to
be true.
Subscribed and declared at Sydney, this 23rd day of
November, 1891, before me,
Also witnessed by A. Balt, Esq., a Commissioner oi the
Supreme Court of New South Wales and Victoria.

On the Flat.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Have you ever, when you went to see the races,
Taken notice of the people on the flat ?
Have you thought of more than “doubles,
“ wins,” and places ”
More than what the betting odds were quoted at.
Have vii'i mingled with the crowds and seen how
many
Were uue men who take advantage of the day
To annex an honest or dishonest penny?
Have you heard the “roll and sausage ” hawkers
say—
“ Come along an’ buy a real ol’ German ‘ Frank-
fort ; ’
Bear in mind, no common sav-e-loys is these,
Ye’re a-lookin’ at the dudes wot sell the right sort,
Any gent as tries ’ill find they are the cheese.
On the course we are the only firm wot ’as ’em j
All the others —we will lay ye ’alf a sov.
You ’ad better keep yer thripny-bits an’ pass ’em,
’Cause ye won’t know wot ye’ve been a-eatin’
.ov.”
Have you ever—just before the start of races—
Taken notice of the rush across the Flat,
When the jockeys ride around to trysting-places
Where their friends await a last selection at,
And the “baited hook” is cast for them that
swallow,
And the bookie holds the line and draws them
on ?
Have v«u joined the blind believing ones who
follow
To discover what the “ hook ” will bet upon ?
They may hear that “ Chatham ” is the horse to
get on,
And the crowd disperses quicker than it grew
As they scatter here and there to get a bet on,
And the bookies take them on at “ three to
two ?”
Have you smiled to see the sequel to such fishing
When the final struggle up the straight is done,
Have you wondered then what Chatham’s friends
are wishing
As the “Titan ” gallops home at twelve to one?
There is humour, there is pathos, at the races,
To be read upon the common people’s Flat ;
In the attitudes, the shouting, and the fac°s
Of the vendors there, you must be looking at.
What an independent style some have —and
clever —
In comparison to us who work for pay ;
You can easy understand that they could never
Have a master,, with their free, unlicensed way.
There are some who look so weary and dejected,
As these aimlessly keep moving through the
crush ;
There are others standing silent and
Scarcely noticed by the gay, excited “ push.”
Have you ever, when you went to see the races,
Taken notice of the vendors on the Flat ?
Have you thought of more than “ doubles,”
“wins” and “places,”
More than what the betting-odds wxre quoted
at ?
Melbourne.
EIFFA.
Since the Country Carried Sheep.
(FOR BULLETIN.)
We trucked the cows to Homebusb, saw the girls,
and started back,
Went West through Cunnamulla, and got on the
Eulo track,
Camped a while at Gonybibil—but, Lord ! you
wouldn’t know
It for the place where you and Mick were stock-
men long ago.
Young Merino bought the station, fenced the run
and built a “ shed,”
Sacked the stockmen, sold the cattle, and put on
sheep instead,
But he wasn’t built for Queensland; and every
blessed year
One hears of “labour troubles” when Merino
starts to shear.
There are ructions with the rouseabouts, and
shearers’ strikes galore !
The likes were never thought of in the cattle days
of vore.
And slowly, round small paddocks now, the
“sleeping lizards ” creep,
And Gonybibil’s beggared since the country carried
sheep.
Time was we had the horses up ere starlight waned
away,
The billy would be boiling by the breaking of the
day ;
And our horses—by “ Protection ’’—were aye in
decent nick,
When we rode up the’Bidgee where the clearskins
mustered thick.
They’ve built brush-yards (!) on Wild Horse
Creek, where in the morning’s hush
We’ve sat silent in the saddle, and listened for the
rush
Of the scrubbers-when we heard ’em, ’twas
“ wheel ’em if you can,”
While gidya, pine and mulga tried the nerve of
horse and man
The “mickies” that we’ve branded there! the colts
we had to ride !
In GonvbibiTs palmy days—before the old boss
died.
Could Yorkie Hawkins see his run, I guess his
ghost would weep,
For Gonybibil’s beggared since the country carried
sheep.
From sunrise until sunset through the summer
days we’d ride.
But stockyard rails were up and pegged, with
cattle safe inside,
When, ’twixt “the gloamin’ and the mirk,” we
heard the well-known note —
The peal of boisterous laughter—from the kooka-
burra’s throat.
Camped out beneath the starlit skies, the tree-tops
overhead,
A saddle for a pillow, and a blanket for a bed,
’Twas pleasant, mate, to listen to the soughing of
tli6 breeze
And learn the lilting lullabies which stirred the
mulga trees.
Our sleep was sound in those times, for the mus-
t’ring days were hard,
The morrows might be harder, with the branding
in the yard.
But did you see the station now! the men—and
mokes —they keep !
You’d own the place was beggared—since the
country carried sheep.
Warrego.
The Breaker.

Column.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
it With no money flying about to show the ways
that “ stable ” winds are blowing, the big events of the
V.R.C. Autumn meeting seem likely to bring prophets to
more grief than usual. The way that a horse gallops in a
genuine betting market is, at least, a suggestion of h!s
chance in races to come, but this year there is no financial
‘ form” to go upon, and the people who “know ” ahy-
. thing are of necessity keeping it dark until almost the
last minute. St. Hippo’s ability to' smother the Austra-
lia!). cracks, would .have to remain a matter Of opinion
until Saturday in any case. If the “wonder” from
Maoriland be another Carbine, he will win the Australian
Cup and annex the Leger, not to mention other races*
but the handicapper has taken St. Hippo’s super-excel-
lence for granted, so the odds against him are longer, in j
the cold* grey light of past experience, than bookmakers
choose to offer. When the numbers go up for the Aus-
tralian CUp there will probably be Well defined first and
second favourites, and the history of the race is in favour
of one of this happy pair pulling it off. Malvolio, Glen-
loth, St. Hippo, Camoola and Redleap, now look the most
likely to become “ dead-birds ” by that time, but their
respective prices (if they all start) will probably give the
best tip. Meanwhile, Tub Bulletin will still hang on
feebly to the old idea that Malvolio, having been a good
horse once, may again run up to his splendid appearance.
The chief point against Malvolio isßedfearn’s reputation of
seldom being able to take many tricks with the same horse.
Churchill, in this stable, won the Newmarket Handicap
three years ago and was a sad failure ever afterwards.
Beverley is expected to repeat the Churchill “ surprise,”
also there are several vague reasons for thinking that the
trainer’s hopes in this direction are built on something
less shifty than Caulfield sand. The Newmarket, however,
is always a mad scramble, which might result in six
different winners in one day, if the field ran six times
down the six furlongs. Wild Rose is very well just now,
and good mares are always worth considering at this
Australian season of the year. Then Buccleuch is a cer-
tainty, although Bungebah has “only got to be in his old
form,” &c., and the name of the animals that have been
specially prepared for this race is Legion. Perhaps it
would be safest to back Legion to win and have a little on
The Field for a “shop.” Nevertheless, a red-hot first
favourite, when found, is apt to fly home alone in the
Newmarket, not so much on its merits as because two-
thirds of the others are “stiffened” to that end. At
tidae of writing the stiffening hasn’t begun* and the
divine gift of sporting prophecy is at a serious disadvan-
tage. One can merely pray that the beer-glasses at the
Plemington refreshment bars will run less than nine to
the bottle, so that drowning disappointment in drink may
not come as expensive as it did last November.
* # »
The free show at Sydney Agricultural Ground
last week, when the trotters Mystery and Rita met, drew
a big crowd. In the first heat, the owner and driver of
Mystery, a grey-headed, innocent, fatherly-looking, fresh-
coloured septuagenarian, who knew a thing or two, let his
mare break, and ere she was steadied Rita get a strong
lead. Mystery was within a sulky length of being dis-
tanced, when Rita would have been the wiffher right out.
Mystery won the second heat, but not till the last half of
the decider did the Melb. pacer show her real form. Rita
was quickest off, and Mystery hung at her quarter for the
first half, when her driver shook her up, %nd, fairly leaving
Rita as if anchored, she finished with a magnificent burst
in 2.29J —the Australian record; whereupon the fresh-
coloured, ingenuous grey-beard received quite an ovation.
Rita trotted quite up to form, and never made a mistake,
but was utterly outclassed by the slashing mare from
across the border. It is to be hoped this match will give
a fillip to sport, now at a low ebb in Sydney. A sweep-
stakes between Violetta Junior, Harry Knight’s imported
J.H. and Mystery would prove a big draw.
* # •
What price the Sydney galloway, Streak o’
Light? At the V.A.T.C. meeting on Saturday, with 7st.
101 b. up, she ran the syndicate’s Vestatia, Bst., to a head
for second place in the Oakleigh Purse, won by Omatra.
That Mr. Hoy should have shown his hand amongst the
horses, ere making more use of the mare in her own class,
is inexplicable. The Oakleigh Plate, won by Lord Hope-
toun, was run a quarter of a second faster than the Purse,
and no better gauging of Streak o’ Light’s phenomenal
performance can be given than the fact that Autonomy
could only run within four lengths of Lord Hopetoun,
1.12, whilst Streak ran within 1J lengths of Omatra, LI2J.
• * #
Mr. Corbett created a sensation at Lady
Robinson’s Beach, Sydney, on Saturday. Thrown into
the water tied up in a weighted sack, he out his way out,
and in about 30 sec. came to the surface with a lighted
cigar in his mouth.
Daring the afternoon’s sports, Fritz Von
Hammer made a most graceful dive from a height of some
70 feet. There is nothing to be gained, however, by
these foolhardy exhibitions, and as with lion-tamers it is
merely a matter of time before the high diver (and the
sack man) comes to grief.

Unless the Australian Eleven soon start away,
for very shame sake they will be compelled to take Calla-
way and leave Walter Giffen behind. Callaway scored
126, at Sydney, against the Northern N.S.W. team ; and
on Saturday, in the Zingari match, he ran up 111, notout,
in about an hour and a half, whilst his bowling average
was 5 for 34, as against Coningham’s 3 for 39. In Adelaide,
on Saturday, Walter Giffen made nil I

Mrs. Alick Bannerman will, as usual, accom-
pany her stonewalling husband to England. Some
Australian cricketers have not taken a wife with them,
but—but let that pass.
# * »
The “scientific” section of Sydney cricketers
object to Coningham and Graham’s inclusion—they are
such “ rough players.” But rough players are needed in
Sydney—men who will send the first long-hop for four.
*• • »
Graham has signed for a fifteenth share of the
proceeds in the coming Australian Eleven’s English tour,
but whether he has promised any part of his share to
other persons is unascertainable, v
* # *
Coningham says he is to get a fifteenth share of
the profits of the coining English tour. He has been laid
£5O to oto beat Turner for the bowling average. Many
good judges think that, given equal opportunities, he
will probably do this,
* * *
The discussion about “ George Giffen’s brother
Walter” has taken a fresh turn. At Adelaide Oval
recently George was “ barracked ” by the crowd ft r keep-
ing himself on bowling, and not giving somebody else a
show, but when one of the crowd cried out, “put on the
Australian eleven man—your little brother Walter,”
George stopped the game and searched both pavilions,
accompanied by a pugilistic player, with the object of
having the offenders ‘‘knocked out.” George’s anger
cooled, however, and the game was resumed.
« • *
The stewards of Adelaide Racing Club decline
to proceed against their late secretary—an ex-detective,
by the way. Some of the members are most indignant at
this.
# # •
At a recent Sydney card-party were playing
two Frenchmen and an Englishman. Poker was the
game, and some wonderful hands appeared. One round
the pool reached nearly £5O. Then—lst Frenchman:
“I have four queens.” 2nd Frenchman: “Stay, my
friend, I have four kings.” Englishman (throwing down
four aces): “Ha!" Then he took the pool,

One of the English sneak-thief papers under-
takes to pay £lOO to the next of kin of any football player
who meets his death whilst playing football, providing he
is the possessor of a current number of the literary
“ fence *’ in question. This, we suppose, is a measure to
increase the popularity of football.

Pauline, winner of the last Hobart Cup, is a
“goer” whose parentage is wrapped in the darkest
oblivion. Her win at Elwick made a pot of money for the
owner, the straight-going J. B. Curran, a Hobart
auctioneer, who picked her up in a saleyard for £5O.

Some seven years ago a strange thing happened to the lady
whose photo, appeals herewith; whereby she became
-A. MYSTERY
Which no One cah explain bV account tor So fat, not bven her
husband. How it happened was this : She lay dying of a compli-
cation of diseases ; often She would faint off, and lie unconscious
as long as 30 hours at a Stretch —frigid as a corpse and apparently
dead. The leading medical men of Melbourne were in attendance,
often at a cost of Five Guineas a day, yet she sank lower and
lower. Everything that medical science could suggest was tried ;
even morphia failed to give enough ease for two hours sleep

A Harvest-time Idyll!
(FOR BULLETIN.)

■“ Do Oi fwant a job? Well, yez—let me see
Fwhat may the worrk and the wahges be .
“ It’s binding my oats, and if you are good
I’ll give you eight shillings a day and your food.”
“It’s a bargain, old man; and fwhen will we
start?”
“Straight off; you can chuck your swag 111 the
cart.”
“Oi will. But hould on ; fwhat are yez about ;
Surely, old man, you’re going to shout ?”
i f r
To make sure of a man they were scarce about
here— _
He shouted four whiskies and then a long beer.
And then he said, begging : “Come on now, my
man .
Let us get home just as quick as we can.”
But, to the, unfortunate farmer’s surprise,
The binder sang out, in a hiccoughing voice:
“Now listen, all cookies, to this, my advice —
It’s the truth Oi’m tellin’ yea, moind—
‘Niver put in more oats thin yourself ye can
boind !’ ”

Thackeray’s version of his first meeting
with Charlotte Bronte. The tiny, intense creature
had idealized Thackeray, personally unknown to
her, with a passion of idealization. “Behold, a
lion cometh up out of the North !” she quoted,
under her breath, as Thackeray entered the draw-
ing-room. Some one repeated it to him. “O
Lord !” said Thackeray ; “ and I am nothing but
a poor devil of an Englishman, ravenous for my
dinner!” At dinner, Miss Bronte was placed
opposite Thackeray, by her own request. “ And
I had,” said he, “the miserable humiliation of
seeing her ideal of me disappearing down my own
throat, as everything Went into my mouth and
nothing came out of it; until at last, as I took my
fifth potato, she leaned across, with clasped hands
and tears in her eyes, and breathed imploringly :
‘Oh, Mr. Thackeray! Don’t!”’

A story of Japan. Evidently the place
that tho Borndrunk family comes from :
There is ,in the world to-day an entire race of people
who regard sobriety as a calamity, and drunkenness as
Hie acme of bliss. These people are called the Ainu, and
inhabit the northern islands of Japan. They were the
aborigines of Japan, hut were crowded northward by the
present inhabit ants until they have reached the “jump-
iiig-off place,” much as the Indians of the States have
been driven into the setting sun by the aggressive white
man. And they are jumping off, too, at an alarming
rate. They must have numbered several millions at one
saK
intelligence, and the filthiest on the face of the earth,
The Japs believe that cleanliness is cousin-german to god-
liness, and are always paddling in the water, hut the
Aiuu never bathe. At bear-feasts and funerals they make
a pretence of washing hands and face, but not a drop of
have^one'drink—eno'u {hdtOcu re °anyon e oT dipsomania-
hut it will make one drunk, and is swallowed by the Ainu
in enormous quantities. Men, women, and children
appear to he always drunk. I paid them several visits
while .coasting in the Eastern seas, and I never saw a
member of the race who could he accused of being even
reasonahn- so ei.

Dear Bulletin,—lll a recent number
you comment adversly Upon a statement in an
English-printed article stating that spinifex was
good fodder for sheep. 1 have known spinifex to
serve as really good fodder for both sheep and
cattle. Away out far west, in S.A., I have, in
company with others, during times of drought,
frequently set fire to the spinifex that grows
thickly in sandy soil, and after a few days (par-
ticularly when there is moisture at nights, as in
the case of prolonged droughts) it grows up very
fast, and the famine-stricken sheep and cattle eat
it with zest. I don t say they will fatten to any
extent, but it keeps the stock in good heart, and
afiows them to bridge over the first rams, better
™an either salt or blue bush. Another thing :

CADBURY’S COCOA is closely allied to
milk in the large proportion of flesh-forming and
strength-sustaining elements that it contains. It is pre-
pared on the principle of excluding the superabundance
of fatty indigestible matter with which Cocoa abounds
—supplying a refined thin infusion of absolutely pure
Cocoa, exhilarating and refreshing, for Breakfast, Lun-
cheon, Tea or Supper, giving staying power and im-
parting new life and vigour to growing children, and
those of delicate constitutions.
In “David,” produced at London Garrick, the autho 1 '
has invented an apparently new variety of murder. The
principal character is a physician who is a specialist in in-
sanity. Like many other such specialists, he becomes in-
sane himself, and is filled with a longing to killsomebody.
Anybody will do —the maniac isn’t at all particular.
Therefore he invents a new and deadly poison, and
by means of a tubular needle he injects it through the
cork of a sealed medicine bottle into the medicine. No-
body can find any trace of the bottle having been tam-
pered with, therefore when the man who takes it dies, the
law starts out to hang the person who opened it and
administered the fatal dose. It is an ingenious fiction,
and yet fact, as usual, is ahead. More than 2000 years
ago, Parysalis and Statira, the rival queens of Persia,
used to eat out of the same dish and drink from the same
cup because each knew that the other meant to compass
her death by poison. Neither dared to taste anything
unless the other tasted it with her at the same
moment. But at last Parysatis smeared one side of a
knife with poison, then cut a piece of meat in two and ate
half. Statira took the other half and died. That murder
has never yet been outdone on any stage.
When a leading lady takes her ben. at a democratic
Cockney theatre, such as the Britannia, the populace give
practical expression to their love. In place of floral
tributes, they heave pairs of fancy garters ani similar
toilette knick-knacks. A reckless ’Arry has been known
to send a sewing-machine round to the stage-door with
his best wishes for ’ealth and ’appiness.
When a fairly strong play isn’t murdered in the per-
formance we generally find that “ the excellence of the
acting was more responsible for this success than the
play itself.” Managers and actors are the largest con-
sumers of undeserved jam in the whole world.
Henry Herman, the dramatist and story-writer, has
been telling a number of tain things to an interviewer.
For example, he says that, alter creating his characters
he leaves them to work out their own plot “ easily and
naturally,” and that “he could write to-morrow a story
in the style of any living writer of fiction were he of a
mind to obliterate his own.” Why, in the name of litera-
ture, doesn’t Herman give somebody else’s style a show
now and then ?
Herbert Campbell, of the London music-halls, we see,
lately told tin interviewer that “any ignoramus can come
on the stage and make people laugh with filth, but there
are many who, like myself, keep their performance clean.”
Herbert should have quoted a few verses of his Old-time
ditty, “ Fair on the job," as an instance of his pure art.
Col. Mapleson, the Italian operatic man, used to assert
that his chorus habitually returned to Sicily at the end'of
the season, and set up as bandits until they were wanted
again, A year or two ago, by way of endorsing his state-
ment, his daughter-in-law was captured by brigands in
Greece, the leader of the band being an ex-basso in Maple-
son’s company, and a good many of the old man’s dollars
went in paying her ransom.
Yankee newspapers are relating the case of a man
whose nervous-affection causes his senses to he always
10 seconds behind time. For example, his sense of
humour doesn’t appreciate a joke (or what passes for
such) at a theatre until the sixth of a minute lias elapsed,
when his hoarse laugh comes in with an effect that is
usually startling. But Australia abounds iu cases like
this.
Fillis’s lions always roar when being brought into the
arena, which is due to the fact that they are fed on raw
meat.
David Lee’s monopoly of the Melbourne Town Hall
organ is nonsensical. He was all o6*a jelly bag even when
VViegand was allowed to tickle it.
Irving was playing Colonel Kirke in “ A Sheep in Wolf’s
Clothing,” at the Vaudeville, when he made the awkward
mistake of coming on with his Roundhead pants not pro-
perly “ fixed.” People were beginning to turn pale or
titter (as the case might he) when the prompter acquainted
Henry with his little accident, whereupon he coolly
walked up the stage, keeping his back to the audience
and speaking his lines as he completed his toilette. When
all was well the unabashed actor returned to the front
and resumed the even tenor of his way with a serious,
“ what-were-you-laughing-at?” expression on his intel-
lectual phiz. Yet artists like Irving and Sarah Bernhardt
(who will calmly pin her hair up in the middle of a death-
scene) are commonly called nervous. f
W. S. Gilbert has expressed his scorn for the “ Theatri-
cal Exhibition” at Westminster Aquarium. Members of
the profesh, in Gilbert’s opinion (and he ought to know),
are too much glorified while they are alive, without being
worshipped, through the medium of their old boots, after
death. “I see no more reason,” he wrote, “why we
should interest ourselves in the stage properties of a great
actor than in the professional paraphernalia of a great
lawyer, or an eminent divine. A collection of the wigs
of distinguished Chief Justices, or the gaiters and shovel-
hats of famous archbishops, would i.ot draw £5.” Pro-
bably, it is in the tawdriness of stage properties that a
simple public find interest. There’s a moral in this view
of the matter, too. Many a stage-struck boy would be
saved by a close inspection of the tin rubbish which
makes a god, in his young eyes, of the rampageous boozer
who flashes it in the limelight. Fairies’ wands! Why
not have an exhibition of fairies’ wands for the benefit of
the johnnies?
The part of Lurcher, in “Dorothy,” was originally
limited to 40 lines, but Arthur Williams “ created ” it up
to present dimensions.
Lilia Wilde, now playing Nance Yeulett in “ Hoodman
Blind,” was imported by Rickards as a dramatic reciter,
and was years ago a pupil of Rignold’s in England.
Clara Cowper, a good actress, killed in Australia by
association with that ill-assorted “Silver Falls” Co., is
playing at Terry’s, London.

At Poverty Point.
GOD SAVE THE QUEEN.
When to the pantomime you go,
O, sit not near the stage,
And never, never seek to know
The fairy monarch’s age,
But from a distant circle seat
Observe her artless mien,
And try to think the damsel sweet
Is only seventeen.
Remember not that years have rolled
Their checkered course along
Since first that fay, in white and gold,
Bewitched you with her song.
Raise not the lorgnette to your eyes ;
Where ignorance is bliss
’Twere crassest folly to be wise—
Do, pray, remember this.
The prudent parent tightly clings
To youth’s illusions dear,
And still believes her gauzy wings
Are what they would appear,
She was a girl, for aye a slim
Young girl she will remain,
And, bless her heart! she teaches him
To be a boy again.
EDMUND FISHER
Soul Flight.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I am a man long smiled upon by fate,
I am a man whose roughest roads aye paven,
And I have heart for love and heart for hate,
And I have brains to bring my ships to haven.
And seas, and shores, and mountain-slopes, and
skies,
Have made me one with them, as boy to
brother:
Though seals inherited bind up my eyes,
And keep my ears and lips from earth, my
mother.
I hold the hand of many a man as friend,
I hold the hearts of two in warmer bondage,
And I shall hold one love beyond the end—
My love, through Heaven’s joy, or Hell’s
des pondage,
Yet here this night my soul soared forth alone,
Beyond the darkness of the earth’s conventions,
And though my arms stretched backwards to the
known,
They brought me nothing in their weakened
tensions,
The love-strong arms of her, my wife, were vain,
The sweet, small hands of children could not
hold me,
The warmest hand-clasps were a breaking chain,
No bond of man or nature could enfold me.
My hands went out in longing for some spell
Of subtle workmanship to hold and bind me,
But nothing stayed me, and my body fell
Away from spirit, and lay here behind me.
Up ! up ! All bonds were broken, and a flame
Of anguished wonder burnt my soul with
longing.
Then the short silence vanished, and became
Alive again, with life and laughter thronging.
M.L.M.
According to the blushful curate of a
Sussex parish : “It has been supposed by many
learned divines that when Adam and Eve left the
hands of their Maker they were guarded against
the inclemency of the seasons by a sort of natural
soft white down in which their limbs were clothed,
very similar to the plush of the present day.” O !
that some inspired artist would paint a picture of
our First Parents in soft white plush.
We had a curious dream last Sunday night.
We dreamed that the convict hulk, Success, had
been duly floated into a co., as per prospectus in
the Australian metropolitan press, and had, as
proposed, started for Chicago with a cargo of
wool, after being insured at very little above
ordinary current rates—all as per advt. in the
morning papers. Then we lost sight of the old
craft and forgot about her. Years rolled on, and
in 1923 we somehow found ourselves at Chicago.
Something had evidently happened to that city.
There was grass growing in the streets, and
lizards were sitting on the mossy ruins of the
Post-office, and a brown bear scraped himself
meditatively on the end of a wharf. The big
iron bridges were eaten away by rust; the huge
pork factories were deserted; and though we
walked for miles and miles through the great city,
we sighted but one human being. Ho was voci-
ferous and covered with hair, and as we approached
he shook his ten-inch nails at us and gibbered.
After that lie ran away. Then we roamed for
miles along the lake-front looking at the aban-
doned ships that rotted beside the wharves, and
at last we struck a sign of life. There was
a vessel coming in—an unspeakably ancient
bleached, cracked, sun-dried, and rotted vessel of
venerable build. There were festoons of sea-
weed hanging from her sides, and oysters and
mussels were clustered on her planks. She
had one broken mast, and a shapeless sail
made of bagging. An old, white-bearded
man on crutches stood on the quarter-deck, and a
multitude of withered patriarchs looked with
bleary eyes over the bulwarks. Also, there were
about 20 black women on board and a number of
children. None of them had anything on except
a scrap of old sail-cloth round the middle. An
unspeakable reek of fish hung over the vessel like
a cloud, and almost everybody on board was
gnawing a fish of some kind, and the deck was
hampered with fish-bones and festering heads and
tails. We climbed onboard, and then it suddenly
dawned upon us that the aged hulk was strangely
familiar—it was the same old Success which sunk
in Kerosene Bay, Sydney, in ’92. The captain took
no notice of us and wouldn’t was obvi-
ously in the last stage of senile decay. The naked
crew smiled drearily out of their toothless jaws,
and the black women and the children fled into
the forecastle. We went down below, and amid
darkness and a fearsome stench we found the
board of directors, chained up and evidently out
of its mind. The board had nothing on at all,
except its hair, which hung down to its knees; it
was unspeakably dirty and gaunt and cavernous,
and it groaned dismally. The chairman tried to
bite as we passed, and broke his last remaining
tooth in the effort. In a far corner we found a
shareholder who seemed to have a lucid interval;
he was also naked and encrusted with salt, and
he was gnawing a piece of stale dugong. In a
shrill whisper he furnished some particulars about
the voyage. It had been unusually protracted,
he said, and they had called in at sixteen
different ports for repair. The cargo of wool had
all been sold to pay for repairs. They rounded
Cape Horn when they were 16 years out, and then
the board of directors went mad and insisted on
paying a dividend every three months. The cap-
tain kidnapped a number of black women at
Tierra del Fuegoin the hope that marriage would
calm the board’s mind, but instead the directors
grew so violent that they had to be chained up in
the hold. They had been there ever since, and lived
entirely on fish and rain-water. At this point
fearful shrieks rent the dank atmosphere ; the
chairman had broken loose and scuttled the
vessel. We made for the deck and went over-
board accompanied by the oldest of the black
women —a party with one short leg and a twisted
back—and when we reached the shore the Success
had altogether vanished. After that we married
the black woman and settled down in Chicago,
and took in the wild man who was covered with
hair as a boarder. The story ends here.

A Tragedy in Ten Minutes.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I Art considered rather fastidious by my friends.
I presume that my enemies speak of me in
different terms. For myself, I think that lam a
trifle particular about my dress, my appearance
in the streets, and, above all, my acquaintances.
One day, when turning the corner of Barrack,
into George-street, Sydney, by the Commercial
Bank, I found myself on the outskirts of a small
crowd. Naturally, I detest crowds. Any cause
will servo to collect a crowd—a crying child, a
man with his hat off.
I was moving towards the edge of the pavement
in order to circumnavigate the obstruction, when
an excited young woman dashed across my course.
“What is it?” she breathlessly exclaimed. I
did not answer, but some other person did.
“Man in a fit, I think.”
“My goodness! It must be Johnnie,” she
cried. “Here! hold this, sir, please.” And
ere I could refuse, a large brown-paper parcel,
clumsily tied Up with thick string, was thrust
into my hands, and the excited woman elbowed
and wriggled through the crowd and disappeared.
In the first flush of indiguatioh I was nearly
putting the parcel down on the pavement and
leaving it to its fate, but that seemed cruel. Pos-
sibly the woman might have a husband or brother
afflicted with fits, and the parcel might contain
articles of value to her; so I waited.
Nearly a minute must have passed when I
experienced an awful thrill of terror.
Something moved within the parcel!
In that miserable sheet of brown paper beneath
my arm Was a living thing.
Infanticide was, so to say, fashionable just then,
and I broke into cold perspiration as the thought
flashed through me that I held a dying infant.
Nay, was actually assisting at its demise. I
believe there are men who, in such a Crisis, could
have “done the right thing at the right moment.”
I confess I did not. “Police-station,” “ Hospi-
tal,” passed rapidly before my mind, but “How
am I to clear myself of suspicion should this turn
out to be a baby ? ” I thought.
I was engaged to be married at the time, and
the mere mention of my name in connection with
such an incident would be enough to ruin me. I
looked anxiously around. Horror ! the man had
been put into a cab and the crowd was dispersing.
The young woman was not visible. I felt my
collar turn limp; I know I must_ have looked as
guilty as any murderer, supposing a murderer
does look guilty. If I had been accosted at that
moment I Would have oWned up to anything, and
at that supreme instant the aunt of the girl to
whom I was engaged approached.
I lifted my hat id a miserable manner, but she
stopped to speak to me, to shake hands. 1 shifted
the horrible parcel under my left arm, and as I
extended the right the awful thing within seemed
to plant two feet—or hands—against my Side and
push itself outward, as though seeking to escape,
or in a death-paroxysm !
I turned sick and faint, and could scarcely
stammer out an explanation that I was waiting
for a friend in the bank. She must have rtoticed
my strange manner, and I could have sworn that
rny prospective aunt-in-law muttered to herself
something about “drink” as she left. 1 could
tell it by the shake of her bonnet.
But now everyone was gone save the ordinary
passers-by, it behoved me to do something. What?
More than five minutes had passed by the P.O.
clock, and there I still stood, a hopeless, undecided
fool. I admit it, I was a fool, but the sudden
rush of circumstances had been too much for me,
and I, who, six minutes before had been almost
without a care in the world, now found myself a
self-convicted murderer (for the contents of the
parcel had ceased to kick), who would probably
tenant a police-cell before long.
I dared not leave the parcel where I was. I saw
before me a paragraph in next morning’s paper
commencing : “ A well-dressed man, of aristocratic
appearance,” &c. No, I could not venture on
leaving it anywhere just then ; I must wait until
darkness came. But meanwhile? Hours must
elapse. I had engagements. I could not carry a
dead infant about with me to a tennis-party.
As I glanced along the Post-office colonnade a
happy thought occurred. There were large
receptacles for packets along there ; I would jam
the parcel into one of those. I would even put
stamps on it and address it to my worst enemy.
Full of this determination I crossed the
road and surveyed the scene of operations. It
would do.
I turned away full of hope, and had reached the
steps leading to the stamp-buying place when a
voice accosted me: “Have been looking every-
where for you, Mister. Thanks for taking such
care of my parcel.”
It was the woman !
I muse have lost my head. I feebly sniggered
as I handed it over to her, “He! He! Was it
Johnnie ? ”
She looked me up and down contemptuously.
“Strikes me, you’re a Johnnie,” she said, and
hurried away.
Next morning, as I scanned the columns of the
paper, I saw that no less than three dead infants
had been found—none of them, however, wrapped
in brown paper.
An experienced friend has given me his opinion
on the episode. He says that the woman at first
intended to leave the child’s body with me, but,
on afterthought, decided that I might be able to
identify her. She therefore came back and re-
covered the thing, whatever it was, until a better
opportunity arose.
This may be so, but another friend suggested
that it might have been some unboiled cray-fish
giving their last feeble kick.
I, myself, prefer the latter theory. I don’t like
to think of the other life dying out in the blaze
and bustle of Geofge-stfeet, tucked underneath
my arm. Still —three dead infants in one monl-
ing ! Which do you think it was ?
DELCOMYN.
A cool Customer lately suicided at Welling-
ton (M.L.). He had lost his money at the races,
and took a bed in a cheap doss-house. Next day
at noon they banged at the door and loudly urged
him to get up. “ Let me alone,” he said ; “ I’ll be
dead in an hoilr.” When they broke in, the bed
was saturated in blood, and the old man was hold-
ing his wrist over the Wash-basin, which he had
put by the bedside, and was rapidly bleeding to
death. He had cut the radial artery.
They call Kiama the N.S. Wales Mel-
bourne. They have a “ Blow-hole” at Kiama.
Parkes, a week or two ago, said he was
averse to taking office; now he says it’s “a
matter of indifference” to him whether he does
or not. It was a matter of indifference to the fox
whether he got the grapes. “My breadless
’ome !”
Writes a correspondent:—“ Can you tell
me how it is that the N.S. W. millers and grain-
merchants only give 3s. Gd. for N.S.W. wheat
while Victorian and S mth Australian wheat can-
not be landed under 45.? N.S. Wales wheat is
quite as good, so what becomes of that other six-
pence that the N.S.W. farmer should be getting
under the so-called policy of Protection ? The
local price in my district (Braidwood) is—best
milling wheat, 3s. ; other produce, suoli as hay,
chaff, potatoes, oats, barley, bacon, &c., just
exchange for store goods, any surplus being left
to rot.- —W.C.”
Is there any chance of Australians dropping
titles while the craze exists for joining secret
societies, which dub a man Sir Knight Brown, or
Most Worshipful Brother Jones, with a drunken
alphabet after the name in each case? Every
underground boss calls himself “ Captain,” and so
does the master of every sailing ketch with only
one small boy for crew. The present state of
Australian titles recalls the story of Francis
Bacon, who plaintively asked Lord Burleigh to
bother King James I. for a knighthood, because
he was the only plain Mister at his mess at Gray’s
Inn. In the next batch of three hundred Bacon’s
name was included. In S. A., it is said there is
no distinction in being a J.P., but folk are inclined
to ask of any reputable citizen without the affix—
“ What has he done to be omitted from the list ?”
Even thus will it be in the matter of political
“honours” soon, and the sooner the better.
Latest advices explain the temporary
insanity of William Watson, the poet who was
carried off to a padded cell because he stuck up
the Duke of Edinburgh’s carriage, near Windsor
Castle. Two or three days previously, Gladstone
had granted the poor gentleman .£2OO from the
Civil List Pension Fund. This sudden rush of
affluence to the brain was more than he could
bear.
Bill Watson, bard,
When times Were hard
Was ever sane and sad,
Believed from want
B 3' golden grant
The lucky man went mad.
’Tis good to read
That bards in need
The finest songs have sung,
And know that they
Can't pay their Way
Unless their brains go bring.
These lines are sent
By one content
With poor and lowly lot:
No patron rash
Will find the cash
To send him off his dot.
His only hanks —
The Lord he thanks—
Are sweet with wild, fresh thyme ;
While lack of wealth
Preserves his health
There’s reason in his rhyme.
A New Occupation for Women.
(FOR THE BULETTIN.)
The belles of the Block are now able to plead
An excuse for the butterfly life which they lead ;
For their afternoon’s “shopping” results in far
more
Than the pricing of fabrics for gowns, as of yore,
Or the purchase of packets of pins, or of tape,
Or annexing a notion and buying a shape
For a “love of a bonnet,” indulging in tea,
Or walking the streets to be seen and to see.
Though heedlessly, none the less sur.-ly, they aid
In cleansing the streets, for their dresses are made
To trail in the mire, and provided with flounces,
Which gather the dirt and remove it by ounces.
Observe, at midday, what a larrikin crew
Assembles in King-street to bet and to chew,
To prop up verandahs, discuss the next race,
And squirt foul saliva all over the place.
Pray how would our footpaths be freed from such
messes
If our volunteer scavengers held up their dresses ?

This is told of Brace Smith, M.P., when
manager of How r ard Smith and Sons S.S. Go. Each
chief steward of the Co.’s boats was expected to
wait upon the manager, in uniform, upon the arrival
of the vessel, to report and receive instructions.
Thus ; Enter chief steward. To him Mr. Bruce
Smiths “What ship?” “The Arrawa.tt.amac,
sir!” “ Any complaints ? ” “No, sir!” “Very
well, you may go.” One day a chief steward,
anxious to go-up town, presented himself dressed
in Ids shore clothes. Manager (who knew
perfectly well who the man Was) looked up
without apparent recognition. “What can Ido
for you, sir ?” he blandly said. “ I’m chief steward
of the Buninbarcooyong, sir.” “Ohno ! you are
not !” said the manager in a smilingly expostu-
late way. “Yes, sir, I am. I’m Brown,”
replied the steward. “ Oh, no, you’re not!” said
the manager, still smiling; “you can’t be. All
our chief stewards, you know, wear uniforms.”
The man hastily retreated, hurried to the ship,
put on his uniform, and re-entered the manager’s
office. “ What ship ?” said the manager, looking
up. “ The Buninbarcooyong, sir.” “Ah ! I see
now. So you are. I really did not know you
without your uniform. Any complaints?” “No,
sir !” “ Very well, you may go. But never come
to see me again without your uniform, for I am
sure I shall never know you. Good day !”

Travelling.
(FOR THE BULETTIN.)
If you’re “ somewhere east of Suez ” on an ocean
liner’s deck,
On the water not a ripple, on the sky-line not a
fleck;
The weather may be sultry, but the brandy-
, pawnee’s cool—
Going to Europe for a flutter on the strength of last
year’s wool.
You think of all the fun there’ll be in London,
Paris, Rome,
Then across to see the Yankees, and so by Chicagd
hoirie.
With a day or two in ’Frisco; and, til wind the
picnic up,
A week in Melbourne just to see St. Hippo win
the Cup—
That’S travelling.
When yoiir socks have long departed arid the
bolts Ate giving out,
And last week’s run of tucker don’t alarm you as
to gout;
When you’re toiling through the tea-tree in the hot
December sun,
And the ancient skimpy bluey seems to weigh
about a ton ;
When the shearing’s done by Maoris and rates
down to fourteen bob,
And the boss looks like you’d cheeked him if you
ask him for a job ;
When you find your match-box empty when you’re
looking for a light.
And no chance this side the station yon may strike
to-morrow night—
That's travelling;
With the sails like copper* sheathing and the rig-
ging iron-hard,
You are beating out your mittens on an upper
topsail yard,
With whiskers stuck together with the flying
frozen sleet,
And she’s running down the easting, homeward
bound with wool and wlreat —
Or a spell of watergrinding your shivering carcase
thaws,
And you’re sweating in an oilskin as you meet her
flying yaws,
While above the howling rigging and the piping
of the wind
Yon can hear the thundering greybacks coming
roaring up behind—
That's travelling.
When the race is nearly over and the long two
miles are run,
And just inside the distance you have got the others
done,
Unshortened still the sweeping stride and with a
thrill you feel
The strong pulse of the gallant heart that’s
thumping at your heel,
Behind the hoofs’ dull thunder, before the wide,
green track
The straining chesnut at your girth who’s slowly
drifting back;
On the left the flitting railings, on the right the
roaring stand,
The silence of the hard-hit ring, the shine of bright
eyes, and —
That is travelling.
WAIPOA.
Six men have lost fingers in a certain Mel-
bourne carriage-factory during the last two years
—all owing to the absence of proper guards round
the machinery.
Scene—River Darling. Two whalers fish'
ing in a bend. N.S.W. Liar; “I’ve seen the
time when you could get cod weighing their hnn-
dred-and-half to two-hundred in . this bend.”
Queensland liar: “On the Upper Paroo you
don’t have to use a line.” N.S.W. liar : “ How’s
that?” Queensland liar; “They come out of an
evening to feed on the grass, and you knock ’em
over with a stick.” Collapse of N.S.W. liar.
At the Dunstan, Maoriland, a siek and
destitute Chinese wished to enter the Benevolent
Home. His friends urgently applied to the local
policeman, who referred the application to his
inspector at Dunedin ; he handed it to the Benevo-
lent trustees, they sent it to the County Council,
the Council referred it to the original local police-
man for report, he reported favourably, they sent
it back tp the trustees recommending the China-
man’s admission, the trustees decided to admit
him. This was six weeks after the original appli-
cation. Then the local policeman went to find the
Chinaman to give him the admission order. But
the poor Chow had already been admitted—to the
cemetery.
Years ago, some Maoriland districts were
huge peach-orchards, where everyone was free to
fill himself, his boat, his cart, or his basket with
delicious fruit, in which even the horses revelled.
The groves grew thus: The French missionaries
taught the Maories before the war tfiat, whenever
they ate of any fruit, they were to step inside from
tiie path and place in the ground the stones or
pips. The Maories in their roamings took fruit
abroad with them as provender—hence the groves
of peaches, apples and cherries everywhere in the
Maori district. A few years ago a blight ran
through them and destroyed nearly all, but now
they appear to be reviving again, and the heart of
the sundowner rejoiceth.
At Young (a recent N.S.W. Assize Court),
before Justice Windeyer, a Chinaman was tried
for injuring a European by pushing him out of a
cart. The trouble originated over a woman, with
whom the Chow was living. He was found
guilty, and the Judge told the Chinese interpreter
to ask him if he had anything to say. “Yabba !”
he answered —two or three syllables, nothing
more. “What did he say?” asked the Judge.
“Him say,” said the interpreter, “if you had a
woman in a cart—a woman you lub whole lot,
allee same wifee, welly bad man jumped in your
cart, talk to her, and no go out, sit on cabbagee,
throw punkin at you, say he knock your eye out,
you no chuck him out? And if he very drunk,
not able to fall on him feet —why you fault?”
“Are you sure he said all that?” inquired the
Judge, smiling. “Yes, him say all dat.” “One
week.”
